ii
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:5==
Abstract - Danish
Dette speciale diskuterer forskellene mellem forskning og praktisk ingeniørvidenskab, in-
den for brandsikkerhed af mekaniske ventilations systemmer. Inklusive de metoder in-
geniører bruger i deres arbejde med normer og input fra den nyeste forskning. Aktuelle
modelleringsværktøjer som FDS og CFAST er almindeligvis brugt både til forskning og til
praktisk anvendelse og kan i mange tilfælde forudsige branddynamik med ekstraordinær
præcision. Præcision, tid og kompleksitet er dog tæt forbundet, og den praktiserende
ingeniør vil ikke have tid til at opsætte en fuldt detaljeret simulering. Tilnærmelser og
abstraktioner er nødvendige for at skabe effektive og sikre løsninger som kan anven-
des i virkelige situationer. Med udgangspunkt i et litteraturstudie vil afhandlingen sam-
menfatte det omfattende katalog af branddynamik med fokus på underventilerede brande
eller brande i meget aflukkede rum. Nyere udvikling og forskning indenfor brandsikkerhed
omhandler høje brandprovokerede tryk i de tidlige stadier af branden. Som forberedelse
til de tre casestudier præsenteres teorien bag forbrænding og kvælning, samt dimen-
sionering af ventilationssystem og anvendelse af FDS. Anvendelse af relevant FDS-input
præsenteres løbende i afhandlingen med fokus på let-anvendeligt og brugbart FDS-input,
som kan benyttes ved simulering af lignende scenarier. Et parameterstudie undersøger
tre tilfælde af tiltagende kompleksitet. Case 1 introducerer den grundlæggende geometri
og forsøger at validere branden. Case 2 øger kompleksiteten ved at inkludere et kom-
plet ventilationssystem. Konfigurationen (sammensætningen) af netværket testes med
og uden brand. Forskellige konfigurationer af netværket; ingen ventilator, aflukkede ind-
blæsningskanaler/luftrør m.m. testes og sammenlignes. Case 3 simulerer en røgventil-
eret løsning hvor forskellige dedikerede røgventilatorer testes mod stigende tryktab i sys-
temet. Resultaterne viser at røgventilatoren ingen indflydelse har på varmeafgivelsesen
og kun minimal indflydelse på trykket. Tryktabet i systemet anses som mere vigtigt i
forbindelse med det tryk der ses i brandrummet. Fluktuering i varmeafgivelsen er årsag
til fluktueringer i trykket og derved også i luftstrømmene i ventilationssystemmet. Ved brug
af en løsning med udblæsning ses kun lidt eller ingen røg spredning ved det maksimale
tryk. Røg spredes til alle de forbundne rum efter det maksimale tryk grundet fluktueringer,
som er et resultat af en aftagende koncentration af ilt. FDS kan bruges til at underbygge
et system dedikeret til røgventilering hvis det maksimale tryk antages som det værste
punkt i forbindelse med omvendte luftstrømme i systemmet. Resultaterne viser at tryk i
scenariet med lav ventilation og lav lækage i rummene er store nok til at have indflydelse
ikke alene på beboere, men også andre områder hvis røgen spredes. Begrænsninger af
ventilationssystem-modellen ses som øjeblikkelig spredning af røg forårsaget af selv små
fluktueringer af luftstrømme i kanalerne.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
iii
==PAGE:6==
Abstract - English
The thesis discusses the differences between research and practical engineering regard-
ing fire safety of mechanical ventilation systems. Including which methods engineers
use in their work with norms and input from state-of-the-art research. Use of modelling
tools like FDS and CFAST are widespread throughout research and practice and in many
cases the models can predict fire dynamics with extraordinary precision. However, pre-
cision, time, and complexity are interlinked and a practising engineer will not have the
time to setup a fully detailed simulation. Approximations and abstractions are needed to
efficiently create safe solutions applicable to real life situations. Starting with a literature
study the thesis summarizes the comprehensive catalogue of compartment fire dynamics
with an emphasis on under-ventilated fires or fires in very tight enclosures. A recent devel-
opment within fire safety research concerns high fire induced pressure peaks in the early
phase of a fire. In preparation of three case studies the theory behind fuel combustion
and extinction, ventilation-network-dimensioning, and application of FDS is presented.
Application of relevant FDS inputs are presented throughout the thesis with emphasis on
presenting easy to implement FDS input when simulating similar scenarios. A parame-
ter study investigates three cases of increasing complexity. Case 1 introduces the basic
geometry and tries to validate the fire compartment. Case 2 increases the complexity to in-
clude an entire HVAC network. The configuration of the network is tested with and without
fire. Different configurations of the network, no fan, closed supply ducts, etc. is tested and
compared. Case 3 simulates an exhaust only solution where different dedicated smoke
fans are tested against increasing pressure losses of the system. The results show no
impact from the fan on the heat release rate and minor impact on the pressure. The pres-
sure loss of the system is seen to be much more important in terms of the pressure peak
seen in the fire compartment. Fluctuations in the heat release rate causes fluctuations in
the pressure and duct flow of the ventilation network. Using a solution with exhaust only
shows little to no smoke spread at the time of the pressure peaks. Smoke is spread to all
of the connected rooms after the pressure peak because of the fluctuations caused by a
decreasing concentration of oxygen. FDS can be used to validate a system dedicated to
smoke eviction if the pressure peak is assumed as the worst point in terms of reversed
duct flow. The results show that pressures in the scenario of low ventilated, low leakage
compartments are large enough to affect not only any occupants in the fire compartment,
but also other compartments as smoke may spread. Limitations of the HVAC model is
seen as instantaneous spread of smoke caused by even small fluctuations of the volume
flow in the ducts.
iv
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
1.1
Background
In fire safety engineering the threat of fire has usually been focused around determining
the development of fire and smoke to be able to predict the tenability for occupants. In
a fire scenario the fully developed fire, with a free availability of oxygen, has been the
normal approach up until recently. A shift has now happened in the community where
focus on the under-ventilated fire is considered as well.
The reason for this shift is best seen in the way buildings have developed over the later
years. Both in newly built and renovated buildings, a focus on energy savings are be-
coming more prevalent. One of the most effective ways to save energy is to reduce the
amount of energy/heat lost through the envelope of the building. The best (”worst”) way
heat can escape the building is through advection or transport of hot mass to cold. As
such the openings and leakages of the envelope where looked to first. Windows espe-
cially have seen major improvements in their insulation capabilities. Double or even triple
panes in windows are now regular or even mandatory to reach energy demands. Earlier
assumptions regarding enclosure fire dynamics have changed since the increased use of
glass layers in windows. Earlier windows used single layer glass panes and would break
in case of fire. This in turn meant that the fire was supplied with plenty of air. Today, at
least in the Nordic countries, the windows are found not to break during fires. The as-
sumption of oxygen availability to the fire is not a reasonable assumption anymore, and
several issues now arise because of the increased air-tightness of buildings.
Engineers wants to simplify the physics to meet more general application to a problem.
This is also called the level of abstraction which every branch of science uses to be able
to describe real world phenomena. The t2 fire is one of the most commonly used models
of fire growth. Investigations into the pressure development of an air-tight building show
that the growth factor have a large influence on early pressure development. Formerly the
pressure rise in a compartment was not considered as important, at least less important
than other fire-induced issues, because the windows where assumed to break relieving
the pressure.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure

==PAGE:13==
1.2
Motivation
The interest for this particular subject was found when looking at resent research con-
cerning computation fluid dynamics (CFD) and fire dynamics. A recent belief was that the
building fire phenomena was understood and could be predicted by CFD and numerical
simulations with enough computational power. However, researchers and designers can-
not sit idly by as the building industry keeps innovating and changing. The focus on low
energy building has caused the envelope of buildings to become more and more air-tight,
a trend that will likely continue [2].
Making a building air tight means that a mechanical ventilation system is required to keep
the indoor climate up to standard. However, the air-tightness of newer buildings has
created a new problem in fire dynamics which was not regarded as a problem before.
Pressure build up because of heated air from a fire can in some cases create such large
over pressures that inwards facing doors cannot be opened and smoke spread via. the
ventilation ducts are becoming a real concern. Fire safety engineering has seen a ma-
jor rise in the use of functionality-based codes, meaning that verification and validation
of calculation/simulation procedures are now more relevant than ever. Recent work and
presentations by S. Hostikka and R. K. Janardhan [3] (Finland) introduced the concerns of
inwards facing doors in smaller air-tight compartments. Two cases and a test in residen-
tial apartment buildings have been investigated, one in Germany [4], and one in Finland
[5]. Tragically, the residents in the Finnish case died whereas the German occupant fortu-
nately managed to escape after several attempts to open an inwards-facing door, manag-
ing to escape through another. Accounts from the German occupant made researchers
believe that an increase in pressure had prevented the occupant from opening the door
for several minutes. This was later supported by CFAST simulations. Also, from a test
case [3] and [5] video material exists showing firemen having trouble opening a door as
a result of the pressure build-up. These cases and the trend of buildings becoming more
airtight as energy regulations demand better performance, lead some researches to look
back on the pressure development of fires in confined airtight spaces.
Simulations of fire-induced pressure issues such as smoke spread via ventilation ducts
have been shown to be possible referencing research going back to the 1980’s. But, the
actual validity of these are yet to be fully determined as experimental work and parameter
investigations are lacking. However, the practical use of CFD simulations is prevalent
throughout the industry and some guidelines may be needed to get appropriate estima-
tions in practical use when applied to compartment fires.
1.3
Scope and strategy
The aim of this thesis is to develop further understanding into the compartment fire sce-
nario, especially concerning pressure related issues such as smoke spread and large
over-pressures which may hinder evacuation or lead to structural damage. The following
objectives are defined:
1. Summarise and categorise the existing literature on compartment fire pressure build
up, focusing on simulation and experimental work.
2. Use a parameter study to show the practices and limitations of the current software
and methods.
3. Develop simulation guideline work for researchers and practitioners to reference
when doing similar simulations.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
3
==PAGE:14==
1.3.1
Hypothesis
Smoke spread is possible between rooms connected with mechanical ventilation ducts
because of large over-pressures in the beginning of a compartment fire.
- Current regulations have not fully covered high over-pressure related issues in the be-
ginning phases of the compartment fire scenario.
- The coupling of field-models and node-models can lead to numerical problems and/or
limitations of the simulation.
- The severity/frequency of early pressure build-up in compartment fires is not yet fully
understood. It may be that the size of the problem cannot justify the time used by an
engineer.
1.4
Readers guide

sections eg. 2.4. Each chapter roughly deals with the objectives defined under the scope
and strategy section.

sands separator and a period-”.” denotes a decimal point.

==PAGE:15==
2
Literature study
The intended outcome of the literature study is to:
1. Get en overview of the history of research going into fire and pressure modelling in
compartments.
2. Present most recent developments in the subject of fire induced pressure related
problems
3. Find literature describing practical and/or estimation difficulties with CFD and HVAC
coupling.
4. Find literature describing experiments and validation studies for input to parameter
study.
5. Give an overview of the current danish building regulations with regards to fire safety,
focusing on pressure related initiatives.
2.1
Approach and search strategy
A comprehensive report by S. Hostikka and R.K. Janardhan called ”Pressure manage-
ment in compartment fires” [3], summarises several projects considering pressure related
issues in compartments. The report was made to answer research questions arising from
firefighting experiences and norm inadequacies regarding high over pressures in build-
ings.
The following two paragraphs are directly quoted from the report [3]:
Ventilation model parameter estimation: ”Both the model validation and case studies
indicated that the real ventilation networks cannot be modelled in FDS using the detailed
system layout and component level information because the specification of all the system
properties would be an exhaustive task, and practically impossible in other applications
except the design of new buildings, where the HVAC designers could contribute to the
modelling. Therefore, new modelling capability is needed for estimating the essential
model parameters, such as the fan curve and loss coefficients, from the known design
basis.”
Acceptability of new management solutions: ”New solutions are here proposed for
the simultaneous management of pressure and smoke spreading. The general accept-
ability of these solutions may require further sensitivity studies and discussion of practical
implications in HVAC design. For instance, how does the system size/complexity and the
relative position of the burning apartment (distance from the fan) affect the pressure levels
in the exhaust ventilation system and the fulfilment of smoke compartmentalisation?”
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
5
==PAGE:16==
These research questions proposed by S. Hostikka and R.K. Janardhan (and other cred-
ited authors in that project) form the rough basis of this thesis. It is from these research
questions the literature study will be based. In order to narrow down the study, the first
couple of references investigated are from this project, as these should contain the same
subjects and/or keywords.
In order of relevance, the following parameters where used to determine whether the
references were of interest to the thesis subject:
- Title
- Year
- Keywords (if any)
- Abstract
The following list contains the relevant keywords inspired by the references in [3].

2.2
Search results
The keywords have then been used to create search strings in DTU’s own database-
search engine ”DTU findit1”. The DTU database search engine uses 80 international
databases2 and it is considered academically as the best choice over other possibilities.
The results of using keywords will depend hugely on the authors initiative to create fitting
and appropriate keywords that describes their works well.
Using reference managers (such as Mendeley3) can make this process much easier. The
manager directly access the keywords in the documents and orders them accordingly.
Thus highlighting a specific keyword can be used to search for other documents in the
library with exact matches (this is also a downside since Mendeley searches for exact
matches that are case sensitive).
The following table contains the search strings and their outcome, where ’kw:’ stands
for keyword and the apostrophes ”” makes the keyword specific, meaning - only find ex-
act matches but ignore case sensitivity. The ”AND” string command is very effective in
reducing results, yet still keeping all words relevant.

String
Results

2.3
Research on ventilated compartment fires
The compartment fire scenario is a stable of research areas within fire dynamic research.
From continuity equations of mass and energy transport to and from a control volume the
first abstractions of the compartment fire phenomena was made.
The simplest is to assume a fully mixed case where the temperature is the same every-
where in the compartment. Somewhat simple relations between burning rate, tempera-
ture, and pressure development can be deduced from the ideal gas law and continuity of
mass and energy in a control volume of gas. From the fully mixed case the stratified case
was developed. The stratified case or two-zone layer model is based on the fact that
buoyancy is a main driving factor in compartment fires. In many cases of an enclosed
fire the smoke will form a layer of hot gases (burned and un-burned). A distinct temper-
ature difference is assumed between these layers with the smoke layer at the top often
being much hotter than the lower layer. The stratification behaviour is dominant at earlier
stages of a fire and also if large ventilation openings are present in the compartment. The
smoke layer development is dependant on the balance of mass flowing in and out of the
compartment and on the fire/fuel that is burning.
Stratified
Uniform
Tgas
Tgas
Tambient
mout
min
Figure 2.1: Distinction between single and two-zone models
When discussing the mass/flow output of the fire, the distinction between a fuel controlled
or ventilation controlled fire is relevant. The influence of the availability of oxygen becomes
apparent as a major factor in the compartment fire.
In an article from 1997, M.J. Peatross and C.L. Beyler [6] experiments on forced ventilated
fires in a steel lined compartment. The goal of the experiments where to determine if the
two-zone layer model was appropriate for low-ventilated compartments. Mass loss rate
from the fire, temperature, and oxygen concentration were among the primary parame-
ters examined. A noteworthy observation was that for a force-ventilated fire the oxygen
concentration was almost uniform throughout the height of the compartment. The forced
ventilation tests showed a one zone model better predicted parameters such as tempera-
ture and product concentrations. A normalised linear correlation between mass loss rate
of the fire and oxygen concentration in the compartments was developed based on data
from the tests and also data from earlier experiments by A. Tewarson et. al. (1981):

==PAGE:19==
J.G. Quintiere4 and Y. Utiskul did research on compartment fires summarised mainly in two
articles from 2005. Y. Utiskul et. al [7] collects several studies that observe the behaviour
of heptane pool fires in limited ventilation, small-scale compartments. Three distinct fire
behaviours were observed.
- Behaviour 1; Extinction due to filling of smoke, i.e pressure build-up. Oxygen de-
pletion was the cause of extinction.
- Behaviour 2; Oscillating and ghosting flames, the flames would travel away from the
fuel source toward the vents where oxygen/air was supplied.
- Behaviour 3; steady oscillating, the flames would not go extinct but reduce in size
when oxygen was used and after some time increase again when oxygen could
enter the compartment.
The ghosting flames are of interest since these can be problematic in simulations, more
on this in later chapters.
Extinction behaviour was found to be dependant on oxygen concentration and tempera-
ture. A theoretical limit (comparable to the relation found in [6]) was presented for heptane,
the temperature was found at the point of extinction.

French institute of nuclear protection security - IRSN (later also PRISME project) has
done much research on the ventilated compartment fire. H.Petrél is one of the researches
involved in many of the relevant research projects. The angle of the IRSN research was
to better the fire safety of heavy air-tight concrete compartments in the nuclear power
industry.
The articles investigated run from years 2003 to 2012. Of these the most interesting in-
vestigates the effect on the fire, mass loss rate, and pressure developments in sealed
mechanically ventilated compartments. PRISME research investigated the impact of par-
titioning compartments, open or closed doors, larger or lower HRR, etc.
With regards to the pressure, significant pressure developments were seen in the PRISME
experiments [8]. Increases of up to 1500 Pa (or more) at peaks where observed. An
4J. G. Quintiere co-author of the prominent book ”Enclosure Fire Dynamics
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
9
==PAGE:20==
under-pressure was observed at extinction of the fire down to -700 Pa. Both the over-
and under-pressure peaks where connected to the size of the fire.
In a parametric study in the same article [8], the main parameters were found to be, in
order of importance, the fire, HRR (especially at growth phase), the ventilation, air flow
resistance in ventilation ducts, and for the compartment, the lining used, and amount of
air supplied and exhausted by the ventilation system.
One very important note on the compartment fire scenario is window breakage. The dif-
ference between windows breaking an not breaking means completely different behaviour
of the fire. If the window breaks ,air may enter and the fire usually becomes much hotter
as it usually will be fuel controlled. If the window does not break then the fire will most
likely be ventilation controlled. During a fire where the windows does not break a sig-
nificant pressure build up can happen if the fire is large enough to heat up the air in the
compartment.
It is therefore of large importance for engineers to be able to say if windows break or not.
V. Babrauskas [9] 1998, present a literature study, where glass breakage is investigated.
For single pane windows temperatures of 300-450 °C caused breakage. Double pane
windows where not seen to break before after flashover fires with temperatures up to 600
°C.
Today (2020) many new houses have energy saving windows with no less than 2 or 3
panes. A scenario where the windows breaks and falls away is no longer the only sce-
nario that a designer must consider [10]. The window not breaking is the reason for the
current issues investigated in this thesis and recent studies throughout the research of
fire dynamics.
2.4
Experimental research on pressure-induced issues
From reviewing several refrences it seems that research on compartment fires began to
change as energy considerations of buildings also began to change in the early 1990’s
with the introduction of the ”passive-house” [2] (Low-energy, Near-zero, etc.) The strict de-
mands on energy use, gave rise to consideration among researches as it was suspected
that changes in the construction (windows with multiple panes, sophisticated HVAC sys-
tems etc.) would affect the fire dynamics of such buildings. Swedish researchers have
known and researched the phenomena of pressure and smoke spread in ventilation sys-
tems since the 1980’s [11].
Several other experiments have been carried out from then, different researches in differ-
ent countries have given their contribution to examine the different aspects of fire-induced
pressure or compartment fire dynamics. Some of the more notable that deals more di-
rectly with pressure related phenomena are summarised in the following sections.
2.4.1
Swedish research
Swedish regulations have procedures to prevent pressure-induced smoke spread in their
regulations introduced in 19945 with basis in research done from mid 80’s to mid 90’s
(1988, 1992, and 1993). In 1997 a comprehensive report from Lund university was pub-
lished, it goes over the major points concerning pressure-induced smoke spread via ven-
tilation ducts. P. Gordonova [11] presents several models, experimental data, and case
studies to evaluate the phenomena of smoke spread via ventilation ducts. L. Jensen and
B. Hägglund were acknowledged as some of the main researchers refrenced in the report.
5”Brandskydd, Boverkets Byggregler, Teori och Praktik”, Brandskyddslaget, LTH- Brandteknik, Stockholm
(1994) (in swedish).

2.4.2
The FOA experiments
Some of the earliest and most referenced data on pressure-induced smoke spread in ven-
tilation systems, stems from experiments done in 1996-1998 by B. Hägglund, K. Nireus
and P. Werling, [12] and [13]. These experiments attempts to study fire-induced pressure
and the influence of pressure on smoke spread via a ventilation system. The first set of
experiments in 1996 explored a single room with a fixed leakage path. The main param-
eters that was changed was the growth rate and the total heat release of the fire. Medium
and Fast growth rates where tested and the conclusion was that The growth rate impacts
the temperature change which in turn impacts the pressure build up.

A second set of experiments also conducted by B. Hägglund in 1998 [13] explored the
influence of fire from a connected ventilation system and whether or not the pressure build
up would spread smoke through the ventilation ducts. The fire room (also used in the 1996
experiments) was connected to a ventilation system and some smaller compartments.The
tests showed that the fire growth rate, leakage area and ventilation system configuration
had great impact on if the smoke would spread or not. If the ventilation system kept
running as normal smoke spread was observed. By only running the exhaust system
(closing of the supply) the spread of smoke could be reduced or even eliminated.
Following the tests a simple zone model was used to compare the experimental results
with theory. The model was configured such that the fire was controlled by the amount of
available oxygen in the compartment. The tests had shown that the fire would die down
when the oxygen concentration reached roughly 15 % (Vol). The researches where able to
estimate the gas temperature, gas velocity, and pressure rise with reasonable agreement
to the measured data. In the case where the growth rate was fast and the maximum HRR
was 1150 kW the pressure developed as seen in the following figure:

2.4.3
The PRISME experiments
Several experiments during 2006 to 2011 touched on the subject of confined ventilated
compartment fires. The research was conducted by researchers from OECD-PRISME
project. The project had strong focus on how ventilation of a compartment influenced the
fire and how the ventilation network may propagate smoke spread. The applicability of
these works may not directly correlate to a residential building as the main objective was
to test for nuclear facility type purposes. With that said, the theory or application should
be considered as the basics are the same. Under-ventilated fire, pressure build up, and
risk of smoke spread through ventilation system are all touched on in the PRISME project.
A summarising article presenting the major PRISME project results was presented in 2013
[14]. Of the relevant results for this thesis the main points to take away are the pressure
build up observations and ventilation duct behaviour. In the following figure the volume
flow in the supply and exhaust ducts are seen for one of the experiments.

Figure 2.5: Volume flow in ventilation ducts, redrawn after [14]
During the experiments an over pressure in the compartment reversed the flow in the
supply duct, meaning that the fan was overcome by the pressure induced by the fire. This
is the concern which may lead to smoke spread to other compartments. At some point the
oxygen is depleted in the compartment and the pressure now develops into a pressure
drop. At around 300 seconds the volume flow is switched, ie. the fire is now drawing in
air through both supply and exhaust.
2.4.4
Belgian experiments
In Belgium [15] (2018) experimental tests were conducted in order to study the effect of
fire induced pressure. The experiments was done as a reaction to an incident in which
a man nearly couldnt open the door to his compartment believed to be caused by an
pressure [4]. The test compartment consisted of a container lined with concrete with
simple ventilation ducts connected. Main points of measurements were pressure, gas
temperature, mass loss rate, and volumetric flow rate in ventilation ducts. Over pressure
peaks with ventilation was 420 to 750, while peaks of 850 to 2035 were seen with the
vents closed. The tests were used to validate the CFAST model capability of simulating
pressure.
2.4.5
The Aalto experiments
Simo Hostikka and several of his peers from Finland has done much research into the
specific problems of fire induced pressure in air-tight apartments. The culmination was a
final report [3] as described earlier. The report summarised experiments and simulations
12
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:23==
studies done in Finland. In addition to the risk of smoke spread the report also suggested
that occupant safety and structural resistance could be diminished by the pressure build
up. Large over pressures could inhibit occupants from opening doors and even larger
pressure might damage structural components.
An article done by R. K. Janardhan and S. Hostikka [16] (2016) investigated a incident
from 2014 where a test in a series of experiments where conducted in a compartment
from the 1970’s with renewed windows. The compartment had a floor area of 58.6 m2
and a height of 2.57 m. The compartments were fitted with mechanical ventilation tested
with different configurations of open and closed ducts.
One of the series of experiments resulted in a fire fighter not being able to open an inwards
opening door after ignition of some Poly Urethane Foam. Also in one of the tests, structural
damage was seen when one of the windows were pushed out by over pressures of roughly
1400 Pa. The series of experiments intended to mainly study the pressure (among other
parameters) in air-tight compartments with mechanical ventilation. The experiments are
modelled in FDS to validate and test further research questions like influence of envelope
leakage and fire growth.
2.5
Verification and validation work
The article referenced in the prior section is based of the master thesis by R. K. Janardhan
[17]. The thesis tries to simulate and validate both the FOA and Aalto experiments in
FDS. Both simulations showed good agreement between experiment and simulation. The
thesis discusses modeling uncertainties or limitations when using FDS. The thesis found
problems in some cases when using the FDS AIT parameter (more on this in a later
chapter). In conclusion the thesis showed that FDS could predict quantities like pressure,
oxygen concentration, and flow in ducts quite well, if the leakage, HRR, and ventilation
layout was implemented in detail.
2.5.1
Oscillatory behaviour of enclosure fires
In some of the references a certain oscillating behaviour unique to under ventilated en-
closures is investigated. Experiments with limited availability of oxygen in a compartment
with varying ventilation rates show three critical behaviours [7] and [18].
In an article by H. Petrel et. al. [18] experiments and numerical simulations of a me-
chanically ventilated compartment is presented. The article focuses on low frequency
oscillatory behaviour seen for some regimes of ventilation rates. The compartments had
a floor area of 30 m2 and a height of 4 m with walls made of concrete but lined with insu-
lation matts. The compartment was mechanically ventilated with rates of 1.7-8-7 h−1 or
0.057-0.29 m3/s. In the experiments three distinct behaviours where seen for the under
ventilated fire.
1. Steady burning
2. Oscillatory burning
3. Rapid extinction
The behaviours where dependant on the oxygen concentration, temperature, mass loss
rate, and GER. GER or Global Equivalence Ratio6 is a figure first explored experimentally
by Beyler [19] describing well- or under- ventilated compartments. Steady burning was
6Ratio between mass flux of fuel to the mass flux of oxygen into a compartment divided by the stoichio-
metric ratio.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
13
==PAGE:24==
seen at lower temperatures with a higher oxygen concentrations and low GER. Rapid ex-
tinction was seen at higher temperatures, lower oxygen concentrations, and higher GER
than with the steady burning. Oscillatory behaviour was observed in a space between
steady burning and rapid extinction.
A simulation study by T. Sikanen and S Hostikka [20] covers many of the problems high-
lighted in this thesis concerning simulation difficulties with ventilation controlled fires in
FDS. The Article studies a liquid pool fire in a closed compartment modelled after the
experiments described by [18] where only steady burning was observed. The performed
simulations are very specialised, trying to incorporate a detailed evaporation model in a
modified FDS code.
The results showed that the modified FDS code could accurately predict the mass loss rate
of the ventilated fire. In some of the simulations strong oscillations were seen, contrary
to the experimental data where no oscillations were observed. The appearance of the
oscillations where found to be dependant on the grid cell size and the combustion model
options like the auto ignition temperature.

The oscillations seen in the simulations where mitigated by experimenting with the AIT
parameter, where setting it at a too low value resulted in oscillations until the fuel burned
out, and a too high value resulted in too early extinction of the fire. The authors also argue
that the AIT parameter is not accurately chosen as the default value found in literature
because combustion seems to be dependant on the grid cell size in FDS. Oscillations
were seen to disappear with a grid size of 0.2 meter and appear with grid sizes of 0.1 and
0.05 meters.
2.6
Real life cases of blocked doors
Real cases where fire-induced over pressure where an occupant was believed to be pre-
vented from opening the compartment door, have been investigated. One from Germany
in 2013 [4], and one from Finland in 2016 [5]. Two other cases have of doors that could
not be opened by over pressures have been filmed by Finnish firefighters [5]. One was a
fire drill and the other was during one of the experiments descried in [16].
In Belgium [4] An investigation and simulation with CFAST concluded that over pressure
was the likely cause of the German occupant not being able to open the front door in
a newly renovated apartment. The apartment was renovated to meet a passive house
standard which means that the acceptable envelope leakage at 50 Pa is 0.6 h−1. The
14
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:25==
apartment was 60 m2 and had a ventilation rate of 0.4 h−1. The article also discusses the
increased risk of back-draft7 because of airtight enclosures.
2.7
Blind simulation work
A blind simulation model is a modelling scenario where data from an experiment is not
available for validation or verification. The simulation can as such not be directly seen as
valid but may be used to further the understanding of how different parameters change the
behaviour. These are generally much cheaper to do than full-scale experiments accom-
panied by modelling and this is why blind simulations are prevalently used in research.
A Master by M. riaz [21] used FDS to test a simple apartment type floor modelled with
different levels of air-tightness and fire growth rates. The simulations showed that fast
growing fires could result in over pressures so high that structural damage could be ex-
pected. Even with slow or medium fire growths the pressure could get above 100 Pa in
the modern or near-zero building types, which is where problems with opening inwards
opening doors could be problematic. Different damper configurations were also inves-
tigated. The risk of smoke spread was investigated for the different configurations and
smoke spread was only avoided with dampers closed in the inlet ducts and open in the
exhaust, the other configurations both resulted in smoke spread to other compartments.
Numerical simulations of the ﬁre scenarios showed that the peak over pressure is sen-
sitive to damper conﬁguration, envelope air-tightness and ﬁre growth rate, but practically
independent of the fan operation.
In an article by J. Wahlquist and P. van Hees [22] the authors acknowledge the risk of
smoke spread through ventilation systems and discuss several methods of mitigation.
The article focuses on using simulation as a tool for predicting the behaviour of the smoke.
Time and expense savings are considered as simulations compared to large-scale exper-
iments are relatively cheap.
The simulation tool used was FDS 6.1.1 with custom code implemented by the authors
to account for oxygen depletion and radiative feedback. A total of 10 setups were inves-
tigated with varying configurations of the ventilation system and pressure relief valves in
th envelope etc. As with the other articles investigated this study also looked at differ-
ent leakage classes, ranging from ”very loose” (traditional) to ”tight” (passive /near-zero
house). The simulations resulted in similar pressures as seen in other works, ranging
from 200 for the ”Loose” class and up to 1900 Pa for the ”tight” class. The investigation
similarly concluded that the growth’s rate had major impact on the pressure development,
with the fast fire being the worst. The authors argued that ultra fast fire growth resulted
in such high pressures, so quickly that structural damage to windows etc. would happen.
in their model they would open a path to ambient when the pressure in the room reached
3000 Pa creating an instant pressure relief. With only a fast fire growth the pressure
would keep at high values for longer not resulting in any structural damages meaning that
smoke spread was more prevalent in these cases. In most of the simulations done an
oscillating pattern would emerge for many of the medium to fast growth rates after the
peak pressure had expired. The validity of these oscillations were not investigated further
but discussed as important to study further if they where not the result of some coupled
model interactions.
7When a sudden supply of oxygen causes explosive combustion of confined super heated gasses.

2.8
Summary of the literature study
The following is seen as important factors by research in identifying the compartment fire
phenomena and how the impact of containing the fire in a room may change its behaviour.
The oxygen depletion and its impact on mass loss rate, heat release rate, and temper-
ature. A decrease in oxygen impacts the combustion processes and alters the yields of
smoke, soot, CO, and other incomplete combustion products. In many of the references
the oxygen concentration is used as a criteria for extinction.
The behaviour of the fire seen in Utiskul and Quintiere’s work [7], and also in the work
of H. Petrel et. al. [18], seems very interesting to the average engineer. The engineer
would have to make decisions on when the fire could develop these behaviours as they
are imperative to the smoke, temperature and pressure developments.
There is indeed a large difference in, steady burning, rapid extinction, or even oscillatory
flames. Modelling a design fire in an under ventilated compartment is much more complex
than for the well established compartment with large openings.
The extinction behaviour was seen when an over-pressure would not allow any new air
to enter the compartment. The article refers to this as ”filling the compartment (with hot
gases etc.)” where both of the vents had a mass-flow out of the compartment. With this be-
haviour extinction happened before any new oxygen could enter and the ghosting flames
or oscillatory behaviour was not seen. In the second article [23] Utiskul and Quintiere
acknowledges that behaviour 1 is mainly a result of smaller vent sizes.
It is of great interest to see if the oxygen concentration drops to the extinction point before
new air can enter the compartment i.e. the mass flow is restored. It is important to remem-
ber here that the vents used were quite big in relation to the compartment size and that
the entrainment of air through the vents where not forced by any mechanical means as
would be the case for a real ventilation system. One can imagine that if smaller vents were
used, the pressure and thereby behaviour 1 could be strengthened, meaning that filling
could be the dominant behaviour when very limited ventilation is used in compartments.
In many of the ISRN and PRISME experiments of nuclear fire safety, the tests used much
higher ventilation rates (over 300 L/s to single compartments) and in addition, also heavier
(bare concrete) and tighter compartments. This may invalidate the usage of some findings
for residential buildings somewhat.
The major conclusion of the literature study is that pressure developments of fires are
not to be taken lightly. Serious real life incidents have been reported, even filmed, and
show that the increasing energy saving demands have introduced problems of fire safety.
Impeding escape, structural damage, and smoke spread via HVAC networks, are the main
concerns of researchers.

3
Danish regulations
3.1
Fire safety in general
In Denmark the building regulations are collected on the website BR18.dk [24] where
everything is comprised in chapters dealing with a civil engineering field. Within BR18
chapter 5 deals with general fire safety, chapter 22 deals with ventilation, and chapter 11
deals with energy and envelope requirements. These three are the main relevant chapters
with regards to this project.
The most basic regulations regarding fire is summarised below, cite [24].
With regards to design, performance, use, and maintenance the following should be up-
held throughout the service life of the building:
1 Materials; their fire resistance and contribution to fire spread are chosen based on
placement and use.
2 Safe evacuation of occupants in and around the building.
3 The building having sufficient structural capacity during fire.
4 Limiting smoke and fire spread in and around the building in the time needed for
evacuation.
5 Rescue services having necessarily sound possibilities of rescuing occupants, ex-
tinguishing fire, and limiting fire spread
6 Use of the building should not contribute to a lower fire safety throughout the lifetime
of the building.
7 Technical installations are controlled, maintained, and tested throughout the lifetime
of the building.
These are the very general fire safety regulations that must be considered when designing
a building. The different statements may be applied in different ways depending on the
building. Geometry and use play a major factor in how the fire safety of the building can
or must be designed.
As in many other countries Denmark has adopted both prescribed- and performance-
based fire safety codes. Similar for both, is the application category that describes the
use of a building. The application category also directs designers with prescribed solutions
or with good practise as per the performance based codes.

The application category can be used on entire complexes, but also on individual sections
of a building. In the case of this thesis the issue of smoke spread via the HVAC system
is most applicable to the highlighted categories in table 3.1. Category 4, 5, and 6 typi-
cally involve multi storey buildings of high compartmentalisation where a defend-in-place
strategy [25] is more realisable than full or partial evacuation.
Because (most) category 4, 5, and 6 buildings are designed and thus assumed to with-
stand fire and smoke spread, the defend-in-place strategy is valid as long as no fire or
smoke spreads from the original fire compartment to other compartments. In these types
of buildings it is therefore crucial that the passive and active fire protection solutions func-
tion properly and effectively.
3.2
HVAC and fire safety
In HVAC engineering the amount of ventilation solutions are plenty, and describing all of
them would be an exhaustive task. Overall two concepts are used regularly, diffusion
ventilation and mixing ventilation.
The diffusion concept relies on the natural buoyancy of hot air. Usually air is blown in
through a vent placed at floor level with small velocities. The air flows along the ground
until it hits a heated object/human etc. The cool air is heated and rises to the ceiling
entraining any contaminants.
The mixing concept relies on turbulence to mix the supply air both heating it up and mixing
with contaminant. The usual implementation is made by installing diffusers (not to be
confused with the prior concept) in the ceiling. The diffusers supply the air usually along
the ceiling gradually falling because of buoyancy.
18
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:29==
Supply
Exhaust

Figure 3.1: Basic HVAC concepts.
The differences between these concepts are quite large under nominal conditions, but
under a fire scenario the difference has not been studied. Research and experiments into
smoke spread via ventilation networks consider only the main parameters like pressure
and smoke layer formation.
In terms of the highlighted application categories the most applicable of these concepts
is the mixing ventilation. This is also the simplest method of implementation into FDS.
Simple vents can be created on the walls or ceiling. The basic parameters like geome-
try of ventilation networks and air change rate should be implemented in accordance to
minimum requirements.
According to the danish building regulations [24] a minimum air change of 0.30 l/s pr.
heated floor area is needed in residential buildings. This is roughly equivalent to 0.5 h−1
ACH. This is the absolute minimum and it must only be used as a base ventilation need.
For most residential buildings the required minimum air supply is usually determined by
the need for ventilation in bathrooms and kitchens.
In Denmark the standard concerning fire safety of ventilation systems is called DS 428 [1].
This standard describes two methods of ensuring the safety of ventilation systems. The
first method is prescribed general solutions with appropriate measures such as dampers
and detectors. The second method is performance based where the fire safety must be
supported by calculations. In the newest issue of the DS428 standard [1] the prescribed
(standard) solutions are based on calculations with the following assumptions.
For application category 4, 5, and 6 without sprinkling and either medium or fast growing
fire (0.012 or 0.047 kw/s2):
- Fire safety measures activate at detection of 72 °C in connected ducts.
- It is assumed that there is no heat, or pressure loss in the duct system. Only pres-
sure loss at the armatures are considered.
- The solutions are based on fire compartments of the size 50-100 m2. Where the
leakage is between 1.0 - 1.5 L/s pr. floor area at 50 Pa.
- For a closed compartment (low leakage) the smoke layer temperature is assumed
to have a temperature of 400 °C and an over pressure of 200 Pa.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
19
==PAGE:30==
All of these assumptions are made in order to create prescribed solutions of centralised
and decentralised systems with standard HVAC layouts. The descriptions of the closed
fire compartment in DS428 mentions risk of smoke spread during high over pressures of
30 to 120 seconds in the early faces of the fire.
With the performance based method the designer should consider the following points in
relation to risk of fire and smoke spread through the HVAC network.
- Pressure developments in fire compartment.
- Buoyancy effects due to high temperatures.
- The air distribution between fire compartment and connected compartments, during
the fire, with effects of density differences.
3.2.1
DS428 on smoke ventilation systems
A short summary of important points from chapter 4 in DS428 on prescribed smoke ven-
tilation systems.
(4.3.1) Use of smoke ventilated systems are generally usable for application categories
4, 5, and 6. A smoke ventilation system may not be applied to application category 1, 2,
and 3 or compartments with ultra fast fire growth.
(4.3.4) A smoke ventilated system is seen as adequate if a smoke fan is used and the
pressure loss over vents in connected compartments is 30 Pa minimum at nominal flow.
(4.3.5-6) The smoke fan can be either the default HVAC unit exhaust fan or a dedicated
fan. A bypass duct must be installed where large pressure losses are present, large losses
are seen for filters, coils, etc. The bypass duct must be open during fire and closed during
normal use.
(4.3.10) The smoke fan should be controlled such that it can not produce under pressures
of over 100 Pa in the connected compartments.
The concerns of inwards opening doors is covered in chapter 7. In section 7.4 it is ex-
plicitly stated that the HVAC solution must not cause pressure differences that result in
forces on doors over 100 N. It is not explicitly stated if this includes the 30-120 seconds
of the fire-induced high pressure in air tight compartments. The master thesis by M. Riaz
[21] investigates the needed opening area of for pressure reliefs during the high pres-
sure. Based on a fast fire in a compartment with low leakage rate (0.036 m3/s at 50 Pa)
the opening area needed to prevent pressures over 100 Pa is roughly 0.03 m2. This is
quite a large hole to have in the envelope when considering heat loss and energy use
calculations.
Reflecting on the hypothesis of the notion that current danish regulations does not cover
high over pressures during the early phase of fire is clearly false. However, any actual rec-
ommendations or mitigation methods are not given. The phenomenon of high pressures
are merely mentioned as a cause for concern that the designer must consider in specific
cases. The differences between an open and closed compartment fire is clearly illustrated
with figures and supplementing text to describe the relevant phases of both fires. Some
initiatives like not allowing smoke ventilated systems for ultra-fast fires, indicates some
notion of the high pressures that this kind of growth can cause.
Several of the recommendations in DS428 4.3 and 7.4 are investigated throughout this
thesis.
20
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:31==
4
Theory and Application
The most relevant theory is presented in the following chapter. The first part will explain
the choice of CFD code. The later part of the chapter will focus on how the CFD code
implements the theory and how it may be applied in a model.
4.1
Choice of CFD code
Several CFD codes exist and could be used in this thesis, however FDS or Fire Dynamics
Simulator is a code specifically made for running simulations where fire is involved. The
first version of FDS was introduced in 2000 and today (as of April 2020) the newest stable
version is 6.7.4. The code sets itself apart from most other codes as it employs a low
mach number estimation of the Naiver-Stokes equations and a Large Eddy Simulation
(LES) of the turbulence. This means that speeds of Mach 0.3-0.4 (∼100 m/s) will cause
numerical instability. FDS is chosen on the basis of availability and specialisation, it is an
open source code meaning that is is freely available to all.
The version used in this thesis is 6.7.4. This version of FDS is available on a general
purpose HPC cluster at DTU, which means that simulations can be done more quickly
and with multiple processes running simultaneously. FDS version 6.7.4 is used on the
following machines, where the home computer is mostly used for trouble shooting.
Table 4.1: Computer specifications

1x i5-4670K (4 cores @ 3.4 GHz)

2x Intel Xeon (12 cores @ 2.20-2.60GHz)

4.2
The compartment fire
In this section the theory behind enclosure/compartment fires is presented. Some promi-
nent works include ”An Introduction to Fire Dynamics” by Drysdale [26] and ”Enclosure
Fire Dynamics” by Karlsson and Quintiere [27]. These are the main sources used in this
chapter to describe compartment fires. Along with the literature study the theory behind
a low-ventilated compartment fire is explored.
For a fire to exist three criteria must be met; A source of fuel, A source of heat or ignition,
and A source of oxygen (air), all of these three have an impact on the fire and also each
other. If one of the three are not present a fire can not propagate (under normal condi-
tions). In compartment fires the source of oxygen is often the most critical of the three,
but to understand the dynamics of a compartment fire the fuel type is explored first.
Fuels are often placed in three categories; gaseous, liquids, and solids. Typically also
growing in complexity in the order given. The combustion of any fuel generally follows the
principle that a certain concentration of volatiles must be present in gaseous form before
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
21
==PAGE:32==
combustion can occur. The concentration is usually referred to as the concentration in
air. Gaseous fuels must first dilute into the air to reach the concentration necessary to
combust, then be ignited by some source of heat. For liquids to combust one more step
is required; evaporation into gas. This means that some latent energy must first be added
to the fuel for it to evaporate then dilute and finally combust. For solid fuels the steps are
generally much more complex. Chemical decomposition or pyrolysis creates the volatiles
that further reacts with oxygen in the gas phase [26].
As seen in the literature study, most of the experiments and data available is found using
liquid fuels. The availability and simple nature of liquid fuels makes this a good choice
in full scale experiments. Other benefits of using liquid fuels is the relatively simple com-
bustion process and extensive theoretical work compared to solid combustion, which in
many ways is much more complex.
4.3
The combustion process for liquid fuels
Typical liquid fuels are hydrocarbons, chains of carbon atoms with hydrogen atoms con-
nected. The complete combustion of a hydrocarbon fuel in air generally has the form seen
in equation 4.3.
CxHy + a · (O2 + 3.76N2) = xCO2 + b · H2O + 3.76aN2
Where a = (x + y/4) and b = (y/2). The number before the nitrogen molecule comes
from the ratio between air and nitrogen in background concentration. The atmosphere
consist of approximately 21% O2 and 79% N2 meaning that there is 0.79/0.21 = 3.76 atoms
of nitrogen per atom of oxygen. If the combustion is complete, or in other words, enough
air is present for all of the fuel to be converted into CO2 and H2O, no other products are
formed. However, if oxygen is not sufficient, other products are formed, such as carbon
monoxide (CO) or other incomplete hydrocarbons. For the simple combustion model in
FDS [28] every reaction is assumed of the form:
CxHyOzNv + νO2O2 →νCO2CO2 + νH2OH2O + νCOCO + νSoot + νN2N2
Where all of the coefficients ν are balanced accordingly to the type of fuel and condition
in the reacting grid cell. In the technical reference guide the simple chemistry model is
explained further with the inclusion of background concentrations of N2, CO2 and H2O.
With ventilation controlled fires, incomplete combustion will be present and the CO and
Soot yields are important parameters that the user must specify. The CO and soot yields
are by default 0 for the simple chemistry model in FDS. For well ventilated fires the values
for these yields are 0.010-0.012 g/g for CO and 0.037-0.042 g/g for soot (SFPE [25] Ap-
pendix 3). It should be noted that it is quite possible for these values to be too low when
considering oxygen limited environments. By limiting the oxygen more CO and soot will
form as the concentration becomes more fuel rich.
There are different ways to describe the completeness of a combustion, usually it is de-
scribed by a ratio.
Air to fuel = (A/F)stoic =
( mair
mfuel
)
stoic
In regular air the air to fuel ratio of a hydrocarbon combustion can be expressed in molec-
ular weight as:
(A/F)stoic = a · (1 + 3.76) MWair
MWfuel
(4.1)
22
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:33==
This is the stoiciometric air to fuel ratio, if one wants to express whether the fuel is lean
or rich the equivalence ratio Φ is used:
Φ = (A/F)actual
(A/F)stoic
(4.2)
A ratio Φ < 1 implies a fuel-lean mixture and a ratio Φ > 1 implies a fuel-rich mixture. In a
lean combustion there is plenty of oxygen to react with the fuel. In a fuel-rich mixture not
enough oxygen is present for complete combustion.
This ratio is crucial in compartment fires as one of the limiting factors is the amount of air
available to the fire. A rich combustion usually gives rise to additional combustion prod-
ucts, un-burned carbon ie. soot and smoke. The combustion or reaction will take place
where the mix between oxygen and fuel is optimal and flames are usually described by
two regimes; premixed- or diffusion-flames. Premixed flames are seen in bunsen burnes
etc. where the fuel is premixed with air.
Diffusion flames are typical for liquid fuels. As the name suggests diffusion of the fuel
vapour has to happen for an optimal mixture of combustion. In liquid pool fires where the
concentration of fuel is high close to the surface, the liquid has to evaporate and diffuse
with the surrounding air to meet the ideal mixture ratio for combustion. The diffusion is
usually helped by the entrainment phenomena where air is drawn into the fire plume.
Combustion of liquids are generally limited by the mass loss rate from the surface of the
liquid to the air. The energy released by a liquid pool fire may be expressed by:
˙Qc = χ · ˙m′′ · Af · ∆Hc
(4.3)
Where χ is a factor that accounts for incomplete combustion (fuel-rich combustion), ˙m′′
is the rate of burning , Af is the surface area of the pool, and ∆Hevap is the latent heat
of evaporation of the liquid.
The heat of combustion deemed ∆Hc is quantified as the total amount of heat released
for some amount of fuel (i.e. [kJ/kg]). For fires the latent heat of evaporation is usually
not incorporated, as the water present is usually already evaporated.
Combustion in reality is rarely complete, and products other than water and carbon dioxide
will be present. These may react further and create additional combustion steps. Calcu-
lating these multi-step reactions can be cumbersome and in practise not feasible. Another
way to describe the combustion is to use the energy released per mole of oxygen. This
is because the energy released is roughly the same for all hydrocarbons [26].
∆Hc,ox,liquids = −12.72 ± 3% kJ/g
(4.4)
This means that the heat release of some organic liquid can be estimated by the amount
of air available. If the reaction is complete the following relation may be used:
˙Qc = (0.21 −ηo2) · ˙V · 103 · ρo2 · ∆Hc,ox
(4.5)
where ˙V is the volumetric flow of air, ηo2 is the mole fraction of O2 after burning, ρo2 is the
density of oxygen at STP. A similar, but more relatable equation in practise would be to
express the energy release in terms of air consumed (i.e. oxygen and nitrogen as seen
in section 4.3).
˙Qc = ˙V · 103 · ρO2 · ∆Hc,air
(4.6)
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
23
==PAGE:34==
If CO and other combustion products are discounted, the heat of combustion in terms of
air for most hydrocarbons is:
∆Hc,air = −3.03 ± 2% kJ/g
(4.7)
A simple calculation of the possible energy release of a fire in a ventilated compartment
could be done assuming: a density of 1.2 kg/m3, a volume flow of air supplied to be 0.1
m3/s (100 L/s), then eq. (4.6) is used to calculate the HRR under ventilated conditions:
˙Qc = 0.1m3/s · 1.2kg/m3 · 3kJ/g · 103 = 360.0 kW
(4.8)
This assumes that all of the oxygen is used and that the volume flow to the fire is constant,
which it rarely will be in a real fire scenario. But, the calculation gives a rough idea of the
size of fire to expect. Running a simple simulation of a compartment with a balanced
ventilation of 0.1 m3/s and a detailed 1 m2 ethanol pool fire, the following figure shows
that it is a good approximation. (see FDS user-guide on ”Complex Pyrolysis Models/Liquid
Fuels”).

4.4
Design fire modeling
In FDS the most simple way of expressing a fire is to define the Heat Release Rate (HRR
in kW/m2) or Mass Loss Rate (MLR in kg/(m2 · s)). However, most if not all references
suggests that the growth phase is of large significance to the increase in pressure. The
growth phase of the fire must be modelled as well. In fire safety engineering the most
common way of defining a design fire is to use the ”t-squared” definition. The t2 fire
describes the initial phase of a fire by an equation defined as:
˙Q(t) = αt2
(4.9)
where α is the growth rate, and t is the time in seconds.
Table 4.2: Standard definition of the t2 fire (NFPA) with examples of application

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:35==
The t2 fire only describes the initial growth phase of the fire, the maximum HRR or constant
HRR must be defined by either assuming some output dependant on the compartment
type or by the fuel burning. For pool fires equation 4.3 could be used to define a max
HRR.
The applicability of the t2 fire should be investigated since the availability of oxygen and
linings of the compartment may alter the growth phase and max HRR. A study by J.
Wahlqvist and P. van Hees [29] in 2015 investigated the influence of the compartment on
the growth rate. It was done by implementing custom code into FDS. The custom code
changed the way mass loss rate was calculated based on oxygen depletion and radiative
feedback. The resulting growth rates showed that the linings of the compartment could
have some impact on the growth rate. For insulating materials the impact was significant
and causes higher higher growth rates than the standard definition. For high heat storage
capacity materials the difference was negligible. A larger growth rate was seen for all
cases, meaning that the current t2 fire may over estimate the time to reach max HRR (ie.
it will take less time to reach max HRR in compartments).
The way one would implement a t2 fire in FDS could be by the following line of code
defining a fast growing HRR with a max of 1000 kw/m2.
FDS definition of a fire with growth phase and constant HRR
&SURF ID='FIRE', COLOR='ORANGE', HRRPUA=1000, TAU_Q=-146/
The keyword TAU_Q indicates at which point max HRR is reached. This time can be found
by re-arranging eq. (4.9):
t =

α
(4.10)
For a fast growing fire with a max HRR of 4000 kW, t =

Another way to model a fire is by using a RAMP. Most of the referenced simulation studies
used this because the HRR or MLR was available from experiments. A RAMP is a pre-
defined curve FDS uses as ”over time input”. The following snippet of code defines a
1000 kW/m2 fire, were the HRR is lowered to 0 kW after 180 seconds.

==PAGE:36==
constant HRR which will try to meet the HRR even after a extinction has happened. The
default value of AIT will mean that ignition happens even if the fire has been extinguished.
As stated in the section on combustion of liquid fuels, the CO and soot yields must be
defined for the simple combustion model in FDS. The values chosen in this thesis are
found in [25] for well ventilated fires. This is an assumption and could as mentioned be
higher in the case of oxygen depleted environments. With complex combustion models
the yields such as CO and soot are not assumed but computed by FDS. Using a complex
model the user is no longer in practical use but in research territory, the use of complex
models requires further validation of the computed parameters.
FDS definition of simple single step combustion.
&REAC FUEL = 'N-HEPTANE'
FYI = 'Heptane, C_7 H_16, SFPE Fith ED. App. 3-4 TABLE A.39-40'
C = 7.0
H = 16.0
CO_YIELD = 0.01
SOOT_YIELD = 0.03/
Complex fire models are not often used in practise as assumptions done on other param-
eters simply cannot justify the use of very sophisticated models. The initial phase of the
fire defined by the growth and max HRR, is of more importance to engineers because
these phases are where factors such as heat and smoke influence occupant safety. In
later phases only the temperature is of much importance to structural engineers. From
an engineering approach the t2 fire is more flexible than a complex and very defined fire,
and creates a larger space of possible applications.
4.5
Flame extinction
As a convenience FDS employs a default Auto Ignition Temperature (AIT) of -273 °C for all
fuels. This means that even if the temperature is below the actual AIT of the fuel (eg. 223
°C for Heptane) combustion will still occur if the mix of fuel and oxygen allows it. This can
and will create problems in oxygen starved environments. The main problem seen in lit-
erature and in initial test simulations are ghosting flames or un-wanted burning. Ghosting
flames are not a numerical or simulation problem in and of it self, as it is a physical phe-
nomena that can occur in the real world. However, in the experimental work investigated
concerning large scale compartment fires, no ghosting flames were observed. It is quite
possible that this is because the fire/burner was extinguished early and temperatures did
not reach the high temperatures required to ignite fuel below the oxygen limits.
A designer would have to take into consideration whether the fire will extinguish or keep
burning to the point where the temperature might facilitate ghosting flames.
A small ventilation area (ie. limited availability of air entering) will as mentioned in the liter-
ature study [7] lean more toward extinction due to oxygen starvation before any ghosting
flames occur. Thus should a simulation try either to limit the ghosting flames or make sure
that the combustion after oxygen depletion is physically correct.
An option that may be implemented with benefit to the accuracy is the AIT temperature,
which is done by implementing the ignition temperature of the fuel used in the reaction.
For n-heptane this is around 223-255 °C. When the AIT is set the fuel will not ignite on its
own and a source of heat is needed. There are two ways to ignite the fuel in FDS. The first
26
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:37==
is simply to create some form of igniter, a box of some sort with surfaces that emit a heat
flux. The igniter box should be timed carefully by iteration of size, heat flux, and duration
of ignition. This is usually done by simulating several times to find a ”sweet spot” where
continued ignition and propagation of the flame is sustained. A new feature of FDS version
6.7.4 is a form of igniter box that can be created by introducing AIT_EXCLUSION_ZONE. This
is a zone were spontaneous combustion will occur regardless of temperature (default in
FDS). Everywhere outside of this zone the Auto Ignition Temperature is employed.
A few test simulation were run with the AIT_EXCLUSION_ZONE, but in many cases the zone
either produced wrong HRR, or caused back draft like phenomena when oxygen entered
the box where the default AIT (-273 °C) was defined.
A problem with the AIT in FDS is that one cannot be sure if it correctly predicts the com-
bustion in a grid cell. Combustion depends on many factors and by using only the simple
combustion model in FDS the AIT value only reflects one single fuel. In reality many more
combustion products are formed with their own AIT. As stated in the technical reference
guide [30] and [20] the default model of combustion in a cell is dependant on the oxygen/-
fuel ratio and energy (temperature). In [20] it is stated that grid sizes of 0.05 meter did not
fully resolve the temperature field for LES simulations. Because of this the authors argue
that the AIT parameter can be iteratively altered to reflect the experimental results.
4.6
FDS-HVAC module
The HVAC module implemented in FDS is a separate solver that is coupled to the com-
putational domain (mesh) of FDS. C.D. Ang, G.Rein, J Peiro, and R Harrison (2015) has
made comparisons of full CFD and multi scale modelling with FDS validated with on-site
data to see if computational time could be saved by using the simpler HVAC module in-
stead of using a fully detailed 3D CFD model. The article considers tunnel fires where
the domain scope (maybe a tunnel of several kilometres) may get so large that the com-
putational time will increase dramatically to unobtainable levels. By using a simpler 1D
model, substituting a full 3D domain the computational cost and time could be reduced.
The results show a decrease of a factor 2.2 in computational time and reasonable results
when the 1D modelled domains are over 80 meters long. The work might not be directly
related to HVAC systems in general as the usage is somewhat different, but is shows the
main point which is, if the entire duct system were to be modelled fully, the computational
cost may negate the practical use of simulations.
The module uses the following governing equations based on conservation of mass, en-
ergy, and momentum in the ducts [30].

The flow in a duct is driven by pressure differences which can be seen from the momentum

At the interfaces between the HVAC solver and the FDS domain the pressure, species
concentrations, etc. are taken as averages over the cells covering the surface of the vent.
By introducing HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE=.TRUE. on the misc line the zone pressure is used
as input instead of the averaged vent surface pressure. This may sometimes reduce
fluctuations of the HVAC pressure solution. [28].
4.7
Pressure loss theory
The following theory is referenced from comprehensive danish ventilation practise text-
books [31] and [32].
For an HVAC engineer one of the more important steps in designing a ventilation network
is the dimensioning of the supply/exhaust fans. The choice of fan is based on the following
parameters:
- Application, type of system.
- Volume flow, satisfying the application or indoor climate.
- Pressure loss, resistance of moving air in system.
The last parameter is of significant interest to this project as it will be the pressure losses in
the ducts that may allow or hinder significant smoke spread together with the configuration
of smoke dampers.
The basics of pressure losses in ducts are derived using Bernoulli’s equation employing
constant mass continuity.
ps + pd + ρgh = constant
(4.14)
Where, ps is the static pressure and pd = 1
2ρv2 is the nominal dynamic pressure at some
cross section of the duct. As the mass is assumed constant, the state of the fluid at some
place in the duct can be found as follows:
ps1 + pd1 + ρgh1 = ps2 + pd2 + ρgh2 + Hl
(4.15)
Dividing both sides with ρg a more recognisable form of the equation can be found. The
term Hl, named the ’headloss’ is the loss of pressure or energy between the to points 1
and 2. The headloss is the collected loss due to friction and/or momentum losses from
change in flow-direction or -velocity. Using Bernoulli’s equation, experimental relations
are found for determining this headloss for several ventilation system components.
4.7.1
Friction loss
The pressure loss due to fluid friction is calculated using a pressure loss gradient p∆,
derived from semi-analytical formulas.
∆p = p∆· l = λ pd

(4.16)

==PAGE:39==
Where, Dh is the hydraulic diameter that for circular ducts is equal to the diameter. For
rectangular ducts with side lengths a and b the hydraulic diameter is expressed as:
Dh = 2ab
ab
(4.17)
The friction coefficient λ can be found by iteratively solving Colebrooks formula for turbu-
lent flow in ducts:
1
