The friction coefficient is a function of the Reynold’s number and material specific quan-
tities. It can be determined using a so called Moody diagram. Several experiments have
fortunately been carried out and the most widely used duct material friction coefficients
have been tabulated in such diagrams. k is the absolute roughness of the surface that cre-
ates friction and for galvanised steel (the most commonly used duct material) the rough-
ness for new ducts is 0.1 - 0.3mm (usually 0.15 mm) [31] A.08.01.
4.7.2
Fitting loss
Fitting or component -losses are defined by a loss coefficient specific to the type of fitting.
∆pf = ζ · pd
(4.19)
Where, ζ is the loss coefficient. It is important that the loss coefficient refers to the correct
dynamic pressure. Usually the loss coefficient is determined using tables where the flow
directions in the fitting is shown such that the appropriate dynamic pressure is used.
Some examples of fittings where a pressure loss must be found:
- Contractions
- Expansions
- Bends
- Tees (branching)
- Vents
- Dampers
4.7.3
Leakage loss
All ventilation duct components should be tested for air-tightness. Ducts are commonly
tested at 400 Pa and divided into three classes (A, B, C). The leakage is determined using
a leakage coefficient where the ’worst’ of the three classes have a leakage coefficient of
0.00132m3/(sm2). This means that 1.32 litres of air escapes the duct each second for
each square meter of surface area at 400 Pa.
It is usually not necessary to account for the leakage in ducts as it will usually be a very
small loss compared to the rest of the loss contributions. Another reason not to account
for this is that fact that the HVAC module in FDS can not store mass. The leakage would
therefore have to be modelled by some imaginary vent or additional pressure loss. The
benefit of including this is deemed so small that the effort of modelling the leak would
be wasted. However it may be necessary for further studies to explore the impact since
pressure peaks of 400 Pa or more are seen in many of the simulation studies.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
29
==PAGE:40==
4.8
Pressure loss application
In FDS the total flow loss should be found as a sum of the friction losses (in FDS called)
Kwall and fitting losses which FDS calls minor losses Kminor [30]. The friction loss is most
appropriately found by having FDS estimate the loss, as it will depend on both volume-
flow, temperature, and density. This is done by input of the absolute roughness ϵ (k
in danish refrence). FDS employs an approximation to the friction loss [30]. FDS will
therefore be able to account for the change in parameters and produce more reliable
results. The friction loss is accounted for by FDS if the ROUGHGNESS is set to some value
eg. 0.15 mm.
The minor losses (fittings) vary depending on the type of fitting, connection or appliance
(ie. filters, coils, etc.) that is present in the duct system. Most manufactures will give
specific losses for a product and this is usually the way a designer would acquire informa-
tion. Collecting the information may be an extensive task depending on the system. The
information can usually be shared between the fire and HVAC engineers, this is easiest if
the ventilation system is designed from the ground up and not renovated.
If the pressure loss and nominal flow of the system is known the loss coefficient Kminor
can be found. Rearranging the last term in eq. (4.13) gives:
K = 2∆p
ρu2
(4.20)
An example is given below using eq. (4.20) where a vent supplying a room with fresh air
will be dimensioned to have a pressure loss of 30 Pa at a nominal flow speed of 2.5 m/s.
K =
2 · 30
1.2 · 2.52 = 8
(4.21)
The dimensionless factor that should be given to the node or the duct is thus 8. The
following snippet of code defines a duct with a fan, a supply vent node, and an ambient
node. The naming of nodes and ducts will be explained further in the next chapter.
FDS definition of nodes and ducts
---NODES
&HVAC TYPE_ID='NODE',ID='NODE_A',
DUCT_ID='DUCT_BA',VENT_ID='VENT_A',LOSS=8,8/
&HVAC TYPE_ID='NODE',ID='NODE_B',
DUCT_ID='DUCT_BA', AMBIENT=.TRUE.,XYZ=...,LOSS=1,1/
---DUCTS
&HVAC TYPE_ID='DUCT', ID='DUCT_BA',
NODE_ID='NODE_B','NODE_A',
FAN_ID='SUPPLY_FAN',
DIAMETER=0.160, LENGTH=1,LOSS=1,1, ROUGHNESS=0.0015/
---VENT
&VENT ID='VENT_A',SURF_ID='HVAC',COLOR='BLUE',XB=.../
30
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:41==
4.8.1
The LOSS input and Tee’s
The loss coefficient K can be implemented as seen in the prior code snippet. If more than
two ducts connect to a node (ie. a Tee) the LOSS input must be implemented in a slightly
different way. One must be careful when defining the losses which can be understood
from the technical reference guide:
”The losses need to be applied in a manner that represents the flow conditions within the
node. For example, if a tee has flow into one leg and out of two legs, it would not make
sense to apply the loss to the upstream leg as there would be no way to distinguish losses
due to any changes of the flow splitting in the downstream legs” [30].
Consider the following illustration of a tee with branching flow.
1
2
3
Figure 4.2: Simple tee with branching flow.
At normal conditions the flow may have the direction as seen on the figure. A HVAC
designer needs only include two pressure losses in this condition.
- Loss from flow through 1 →3
- Loss from flow through 1 →2
If however a fire develops in a room connected to either duct 2 or 3, the flow may change
direction and depending on the volume flow in either direction the pressure loss will also
change. This means that a fire engineer facing this exact tee will have to account for 6
possible flow conditions.
- Loss from flow through 1 →3
- Loss from flow through 1 →2
- Loss from flow through 2 →1
- Loss from flow through 2 →3
- Loss from flow through 3 →1
- Loss from flow through 3 →2
This and the fact that pressure loss in tee’s are dependant on the flow ratio between each
duct, makes the task exhaustive and not practical. Another implication is that the loss
factors for 2 →3 and 3 →2 are not usually given in tables or data-sheets and must be
approximated.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
31
==PAGE:42==
For a tee node in FDS the loss must be defined as follows assuming the same fitting as
in fig. 4.2.
FDS definition of a Tee node
&HVAC TYPE_ID='NODE',ID='TEE',
DUCT_ID='DUCT_1','DUCT_2','DUCT_3',XYZ=...,
LOSS(1,1)=0
,LOSS(1,2)=K12,LOSS(1,3)=K13,
LOSS(2,1)=K21,LOSS(2,2)=0
,LOSS(2,3)=K23,
LOSS(3,1)=K31,LOSS(3,2)=K32,LOSS(3,3)=0/
This is essentially a matrix where the appropriate K factor should be used as input. The
logic is as follows:
- LOSS(1,3) = Loss from DUCT_1 →DUCT_3
- LOSS(1,2) = Loss from DUCT_1 →DUCT_2
- and so on...
The diagonal should be zero if the loss of the duct is defined elsewhere eg. the duct
itself. Naming of the ducts DUCT_1,DUCT_2,... does not matter, the matrix is defined in
the listed order of DUCT_ID.
The losses are assumed minor in comparison to other losses in the system, this is not
saying that this field can be ignored. The task of implementing correct losses for all flow
conditions are simply too exhaustive a task for the precision gained. The volume flow is
only mainly balanced by the vent losses and the area of the ducts connected to the tee
node. In order to do this the losses in all directions of the tee are set equal eg. 0 or 1.
The impact of not including the correct tee losses are not large, but can be significant
when the branching duct is much smaller in cross section compared to the main duct, or
if the flow is very unbalanced in some other vay [31]. As a test one might try to simulate
a tee node in FDS and look at the implications of not including the 6 losses.
4.9
Envelope leakage application
Since leakage in buildings is often a sub grid phenomena the leakage has to be modelled
by other means. Two of the most prominent methods present in FDS are the ”bulk” and
”localised” -leakage methods. Both of these methods uses a leakage area. The area may
be determined from the following relation which is based on flow through a duct orifice.
˙Vleak = ALsign(∆p)
√
2|∆p|
ρ∞
(4.22)
The standard way of testing leakage is to use the so called ”Fan-blower” test. Where a
fan is usually installed in an exterior doorway, creating an over pressure inside the house
of 50 Pa. From the volume flow and pressure of the fan the leakage can be determined.
This means that ˙Vleak and ∆p are known in eq. (4.22). The leakage area can then be
found according to the danish building regulation, where the limiting leakage is determined
based on the floor area.

The simplest way to define bulk leakage is to define the leak to go from the created zone
to ambient (zone 0). This is done by adding the LEAK_PATH parameter to an external
surface. Setting LEAK_PATH=1,0 means that the leak is flowing from zone 1 to zone 0:
FDS definition of bulk zone leakage
&ZONE XB=..., LEAK_AREA(0)=0.0055/
&SURF ID='LEAKY EXTERIOR WALL', LEAK_PATH=1,0/
The localised leak method is intended to be used where cracks or openings are known,
like cracks under doors or around windows. The localised method does not use the zone
pressure but the local surface averaged pressure. This method is preferred if stratifica-
tion is assumed to have an impact on the leakage flow as it will better account for the
temperature loss/gain due to advection of air.
FDS definition of localised leakage
&SURF ID='INTERNAL',DEFAULT=.TRUE./
&VENT XB=...,SURF_ID='INTERNAL',ID='LEAK_VENT',COLOR='BLUE'/
&HVAC ID='LEAK',TYPE_ID='LEAK',
VENT_ID='LEAK_VENT',VENT2_ID='AMBIENT',AREA=0.0055/

5
Parameter study
5.1
Defining the goals
The most important factor is the size and course of the fire and one should ask the practical
questions:
Is the oxygen depletion relevant?
Is the pressure peak and/or pressure drop relevant?
Are all of the HVAC network pressure losses relevant?
Is a physically correct placement of the fire relevant?
How much time is available?
These questions together with the literature study and danish regulations lead to some
concerns of the limitations with the current modelling capabilities. FDS can in fact sim-
ulate very detailed fires given the correct input and by using more detailed combustion
models. However, the amount of time and/or knowledge of the program and physical phe-
nomena is not always realisable. Shortcomings of the user guide and shortcomings or
differences of the users may lead to different assumptions, or in severe cases, errors that
lead to incorrect solutions. In a study by N. Johansson [33] simulations are compared by
making different users simulate the same case in FDS. Eight practising engineering firms
were tasked to simulate a case and give back pre-defined output. Large discrepancies
were seen between the participants. Different assumptions, mistakes, insufficient knowl-
edge about FDS or about fire dynamics, all contributed to variations and mistakes in the
solutions. The findings in the study are quite worrying, and should be taken seriously in
regulations that allow documentation by use of simulation programs.
Pressure related issues due to low leakage of the envelope of smaller compartments
is a relatively new subject in fire dynamics. While not applicable to every practitioner the
study by [33] shows that guidelines in specialised (even simple) cases might become very
helpful in the future where FDS most likely will increase in use, size and complexity. The
Parameter study together with the literature study will be used to form a basic guideline
with practises of simulating pressure related issues in under-ventilated compartment fires.
The guide will give practitioners a helping hand in determining what input might be useful
to mitigating problems.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
35
==PAGE:46==
5.2
Choice of parameters
The several tests done prior to the actual parameter analysis have given some insight
into how the study must be carried out. It has also given insight into what problems may
occur when trying to simulate such closed compartments as has been described. The
default value of AIT has proven to complicate things when trying to model a design fire.
If the design fire is created with a constant HRR after a t2 growth, the combustion model
in FDS will try to reach the given max HRR. But, if the oxygen in the room is not sufficient
the combustion will drop before the intended max HRR is reached.
At this point the designer must choose to go in one of two directions:
- Keep it simple and have unwanted burning at the vents that may contribute to oscil-
lations of the pressure, thus impacting the flow in the ventilation network.
- Create a detailed fire (as in [20]) where exact parameters of the fuel is taken into
account, such as type of fuel, burnout due to oxygen or fuel depletion, impact of
radiation to the pool surface, etc.
The first direction is best in terms of scaleability and flexibility, but the current combustion
model of FDS somewhat limits the use of the simpler design fire at the point of oxygen
depletion. In this thesis the focus lies on the smoke spread due to the pressure build-up
in the compartment, and the focus should lie on the pressure peaks. The oscillations
due to burning at the vents should be limited as the experiments and detailed simulations
does not show ghosting flames. Whether this is due to the experimental setups where
some control of the flame was employed is not easy to say. By using the simple combus-
tion model the user has to take into consideration that the output of the fire after oxygen
depletion may not be modelled correctly and the physical location of the fire is uncertain.
In an engineering sense the later choice is too restrictive in the way the it limits the fire
to one very detailed fuel with a specific size and growth based on several factors. The
benefit of using a more detailed fire and fuel is that the observed phenomena are more
believable.
The leakage of the envelope is stated as a very important parameter by several of the
references. This is also why many of the theses investigate different leakage values by
means of parameter studies. Especially by the many references from Aalto university, who
consider the leakage to be one of the main parameters. From several of the parameter
studies the impact of the leakage area can be seen. As a result this project will not try to
replicate these findings, but instead go in another direction. The default leakage method
and area chosen in the following parameter study is the danish normal-class leakage
defined in table 4.3 by means of the bulk leakage model at an air change of 1.5h−1.
As well as the leakage model the t2 fire growth model has also been extensively inves-
tigated in many of the references. The default fire model in the parameter study will be
the fast growing fire, as this seems most appropriate for the multi floor apartment building
case. In one case an ultra fast and medium fire is simulated as well for comparison to
reference data.
The parameter study will therefore try to enlighten with regards to different HVAC con-
figurations as they are normally seen in multistory apartment buildings. Different cases
will test the robustness of a simple yet realistic ventilation system, exploring worst-case
scenarios. The first couple of cases will try to locate problems with simple setups and
give inspiration to a final case where everything is collected.
36
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:47==
6
Parameter study: Case 1
This case will be used to study the mesh independence of the solution. Starting with three
mesh sizes the solution is compared by plotting several of the relevant parameters. Only
one room will be meshed, the second will be mimicked by having HVAC vents connecting
to ambient conditions. After the mesh independence study, a mesh for the connected
room will be created and tested with two different mesh sizes to study the impact on the
computational time.
After the mesh independence study, several other test are performed on the setup, testing
the capabilities of FDS. These include: design fire testing, leakage method testing, HVAC
network pressure loss and general testing, etc. A quick comparison between a simple
zone model and the results from FDS is also performed. These tests are meaningful
as they give insight into what parameters may help or be detrimental when simulating a
compartment fire scenario.
6.1
The standard compartment
A simple geometry with a floor area of 50 m2 is chosen with a ceiling height of 2.6 meters.
This is almost the same base room geometry used in the simulations by M. Riaz [21].
The current layout has no internal walls and no furniture in order to reduce the amount of
parameters. Walls are named according to their orientation (N,W,E,S) and the room they
enclose. Concrete is chosen as material for the walls so the t2 fire can be used as stated
in the study by J. Wahlqist and P. van Hees. [29].

as possible. A central positioning of the fire satisfies this choice best.

Case 1: Base setup

6.3
Introducing relaxation parameters
FDS has some measures to try an alleviate instabilities when using the HVAC module.
Since the FDS pressure solution and the HVAC module are not directly coupled but im-
plicitly coupled by using the average pressure at the node vent, some stability issues may
arise. The keyword HVAC_PRES_RELAX can be used to lag the iteration pressure at the
next time step. Setting this parameter close to 1 will make the solution better at predict-
ing short, transient changes in the pressure, but worse in predicting long term pressure
changes. Setting it closer to 0 increases the dependence on the FDS domain pressure
which is seen to influence the pressure solution of both solvers.
In this case where the pressure change is quite drastic (sharp HRR drop) over a short
amount of time, it makes sense to have the relaxation set closer to 1. In the actual solution
procedure this means that the HVAC node pressure at time step n+1 is close or equal to
the previous node pressure:
pn+1
node = pFDS · (1 −HVAC_PRES_RELAX) + pn
node · HVAC_PRES_RELAX
(6.1)
Where pFDS is the FDS domain pressure averaged at the vent.
Another method than can be applied is the HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE keyword, this parame-
ter can also help reduce the fluctuations. When set to .TRUE. the HVAC solver uses the
zone pressure and the local stagnation part of the pressure at the vent. With the keyword
set to .FASLE. the local stagnant and dynamic part of the pressure solution is used at the
vent, and this can lead to fluctuations between the solvers.
A short parameter study is conducted to show the two keywords impact on a fast growing
fire with the base setup of compartment geometry and HVAC network. The data points are
averaged over 1 second to avoid ”smoothing” over the fluctuations. Left figures fig. 6.4a,
6.5a, and 6.6a show comparison between two settings of the HVAC_PRES_RELAX keyword.
Right figures 6.4b, 6.5b, and 6.6b show comparison between the true and false setting of
the HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE keyword with HVAC_PRES_RELAX set to 0.8.

Clear benefits of increasing the HVAC_PRES_RELAX is seen on the left hand figures. Com-
paring the right hand side figures with HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE true (red) or false (blue) and
HVAC_PRES_RELAX set to 0.8, also show clear reduction in the size of the fluctuations.
Some other measures to take is to configure the tolerances of the pressure solution by
increasing the number of iterations done by the solver on the pressure solution. Simply
done by setting the keyword MAX_PRESSURE_ITERATIONS to a value higher than 10. While
also setting the PRESSURE_TOLERANCE to a value less than the default 2000.
Another place where some instability issues may arise is where high velocities are present.
Velocities over 100 m/s may lead to inaccurate or even wrong values as the low mach
number approximation is exceeded. In a low ventilated compartment the volume flow cre-
ated by a fire may cause the velocity at vents and in ducts to reach quite high values. In or-
der to resolve the flow entering and leaving these HVAC vents the velocity tolerance (size
of error acceptance) can be lowered by introducing the keyword VELOCITY_TOLERANCE
with a value lower than δx/2 (ie. half of the grid cell size).
The best way to check whether the pressure solution has achieved an acceptable level
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
41
==PAGE:52==
of error is to monitor the number of pressure iterations done at each time step. Setting
the keyword VELOCITY_ERROR_FILE=.TRUE. creates a file where the number pressure
iterations, velocity, and pressure error, can monitored. If the MAX_PRESSURE_ITERATIONS
is 10 (default max iterations) for many of the time steps one should consider to increase
the maximum iterations and decrease the PRESSURE_TOLERANCE. The same procedure
can be done for the VELOCITY_TOLERANCE where values of a magnitude larger than δx/2
should not be observed in the VELOCITY_ERROR_FILE.
FDS definition of relaxation parameters and other instability relieving keywords
&MISC ...,
HVAC_PRES_RELAX=1.0,
HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE=.TRUE./
&PRES CHECK_POISSON=.TRUE.,
PRESSURE_TOLERANCE=2000,
MAX_PRESSURE_ITERATIONS=20,
VELOCITY_TOLERANCE=0.001/
&DUMP NFRAMES=...,
VELOCITY_ERROR_FILE=.TRUE./
//Keep the number of dump frames small enough to
//
//capture any fluctuations, ex. 1 seconds intervals:
//
//NFRAMES = T_END - T_BEGIN,
//

6.4
Case 1: Mesh independence
Because of the difficulties with modelling an enclosure fire in FDS, the mesh resolution
must be taken out as an uncertain parameter. Case 1 will be used to determine an appro-
priate cell size for the following cases. It is assumed that an adequate mesh study can be
achieved by looking only at the compartment where fire is present. Meaning that room 02
is mimicked as ambient by setting the nodes G and C to ambient conditions. The criteria
for combustion at low oxygen levels is based on the energy (heat) and oxygen/fuel ratio
of the cell, which is why it is important to reach a satisfactory mesh independence in the
fire compartment.
The fire is defined by a simple vent with an assigned constant HRR. A fast t2 fire is im-
plemented in for the growth phase of the fire. The fire base is 4 m2 with a max HRR of
4000 kW, meaning that the HRRPUA input is set equal to 1000 kW/m2 with TAU_Q = -293
seconds.
To achieve a well resolved fire the FDS user-guide and technical reference guide suggest
following some validation cases created to test FDS. These validation cases uses a the-
oretical description of the dimension of a fire to try and give quantity to coarse and fine
mesh cell sizes.
D∗=
(
˙Q
ρ∞cpT∞√g
)2/5
(6.2)
Equation 6.2 is the characteristic diameter of a fire, it may be used to approximate an
appropriate cell size that can resolve the turbulent nature of a fire plume. The user guide
and technical reference guide gives some rough guides to define a coarse, medium, and
fine mesh. In the following table 6.3 these are given for the current fire investigated in this
thesis.

The most crucial part of the HRR to make sure is resolved, is the growth, the max HRR,
and the point of oxygen depletion. On fig. 6.7 the growth phase is seen as quite stable with
no real discrepancies between the meshes. This is expected as the simple combustion
model is employed and FDS only allows combustion to the prescribed point at the given
time. Observing fig. 6.8 makes it clear that the cell size impacts the combustion model
when oxygen concentration is low. Since both combustion is based on concentration and
energy of the cell the finer resolution will capture combustion more accurately. Looking at
the max HRR reached, the mean is the same for all meshes (around 1500 kW).

6.4.1
Meshing of connected rooms
In this test a mesh is created for the connected room, such that the computational time
and results can be compared. The ambient vents C and G are both now assigned a vent
of the same size as vent A and E. In order to reduce the number of unknowns in the
parameter study the heat conduction between rooms are removed by leaving a small gap
between the rooms. This also means that the meshes (thereby pressure zones) become
fully independent of each other, other than the connection by the HVAC network. This
also circumvents the specific meshing rules implied by FDS, and it creates the possibility
of having different mesh sizes for the connected rooms.
Other advantages of not having the meshes ”touching” each other is the use of the
VELOCITY_TOLERANCE pressure solver keyword. Changing the tolerance will impact the
computational time. If the meshes are ”touching”, the VELOCITY_TOLERANCE might need
to be lowered to increase the accuracy between the touching boundaries where more
pressure iterations might be needed. This is also the case if an obstacle (internal wall)
is present in the room, as the velocity close to the obstacle needs to be resolved more
accurately.
The mesh size of the connected room can be any size that the user wants, since the
meshes are not interacting with each other’s boundaries. One might think that since the
connected rooms do not have any fire occurring, calculation can be reduced on the con-
nected rooms by lowering the cell size in these rooms. But this is not a not ideal since
FDS will use equal time stepping for all meshes.
In other words, timings of the solvers are dictated by both the mesh size and the time
steps. If there is more than one mesh FDS will use the time step size dictated by the
mesh where the smallest time step is needed to keep the solver stable. So, even if one
meshes time step is run quickly, it will have to go through the same number of time steps
as a mesh where a time step takes longer to compute.
It would then be detrimental to keep connected mesh smaller as accuracy could be lost.
With no penalty to computational time the meshing of the connected rooms should at least
have the same number of cells as the room with fire present. To illustrate this a simple
test was run where the two different meshes were used in the connected room. The test
was run on case 1 with two cores and each assigned one mesh threaded by 4 processors.
In table 6.5 below the time used waiting for calculations to be concluded is shown as the
COMM header (from the ’_cpu.csv’ output file). The time used waiting is a reflection of
efficiency, where for optimal cases should be equal to both the fire room and connected
room meshes.
Table 6.5: Comparison of CPU time usage for meshing of connected room.
Test name
Mesh 1
Mesh 2

The 82% of time spent waiting, shows that decreasing the number of cells in the connected
room is in-efficient even if the two meshes are not interacting directly. This is a result
showing that the time steps are being determined by the fire mesh, meaning that many
more time steps than ”needed” are processed for mesh 2.

Together with the HRR and the oxygen concentration figures, it becomes apparent that
two scenarios are of importance. The first considers a full extinction of the fire, meaning
a HRR of 0 at the point where the oxygen concentrations reaches the lowest stable point.
On fig. 6.18 this is around 11-12 % which is a lower value than the table value of LOC for n-
Heptane which is 13.4 % [34]. This is due to model FDS uses to predict if combustion will
happen as the temperature lowers the limit of combustion. The other scenario is where
new oxygen enters the room and combustion of the fuel can re-ignite. This could as seen
in [7] give rise to oscillating combustion.
6.4.3
Impact of surface material
Wahlqvist and van Hees [29] did, as stated in the literature study, similar and more thor-
ough simulations on this exact subject. The purpose of their investigations were to see
if a design fire such as the t2 fire was well represented in a compartment fire scenario.
The simple answer was that the t2 growth in compartments generally reached peak HRR
earlier than with the normal definition. The effect was most apparent if the surfaces was
made of some very insulating material like gypsum or insulation bats. The effect was
less severe (negligible) for thermally thin or large heat storing capacity materials. The
researchers concluded that materials such as concrete could be used with little to no
repercussions. The choice has been taken here to go forth with concrete as the envelope
material to keep the validity of the t2 fire.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
51
==PAGE:62==
6.4.4
Extinction or continued burning
As mentioned in the literature study and in the theory section, three phenomena should
be considered. Steady continued burning, oscillating behaviour, or rapid extinction. The
steady burning must be supplied by oxygen to be able to continue combustion.
With the simple combustion model applied in FDS, the three behaviour regimes of under
ventilated fires are hard to recreate or predict. In terms of the simple combustion model the
rapid extinction of the fire shortly after the point of oxygen depletion seems appropriate.
However, continued burning is also seen and both regimes may be equally valid, where
the HRR becomes dependant on the supplied air flow. The oscillations seen in the early
tests are of much higher frequency than most references have reported. The study [18]
references other work reporting oscillations of 0.2-0.002 Hertz or periods of 5-50 seconds.
The study it self finds periods of 150-200 seconds, finding that the range is relatively large.
A specialised design fire is made in this section to test how a designer could implement
either continued burning or rapid extinction. The simple fast fire is already shown above,
this is where the default setting of AIT is used. The resulting HRR development shows
continued burning at the locations where oxygen can enter the compartment, ex at vents
or over entire leakage surfaces with high frequency oscillatory behaviour.
If a designer would want to simulate a fire continuing to burn after the point of oxygen
depletion the method shown above could satisfy this with the caveat of not being able to
verify/validate the physical location of the fire.
A more realistic prediction of extinction may be implemented by including the AIT parame-
ter. The default AIT definition allows for fire to burn wherever the oxygen/fuel ratio allows
it. The Auto Ignition Temperature of the current fuel, n-Heptane, lie in the span of 223-255
°C depending on the test vessel size [26]. But, even by implementing the AIT one can not
be sure that the combustion limits are correctly predicted for low ventilated compartments
by FDS, as discussed in [20]. Five AIT values are here tested with the following FDS
input.
The AIT keyword should be given on the REAC line. The following lines of FDS input
creates a fast growing fire with a max HRR of 4000 kW.
FDS definition of fuel and fire with AIT implemented.
---FUEL
&REAC FUEL = 'N-HEPTANE'
FYI = 'Heptane, C_7 H_16'
C = 7.0
H = 16.0
CO_YIELD = 0.01
SOOT_YIELD = 0.03
HEAT_OF_COMBUSTION = 44600
AUTO_IGNITION_TEMPERATURE=223/
---OUTPUT
&SURF ID='FIRE', COLOR='ORANGE',
HRRPUA=1000, TAU_Q=-293, TMP_FRONT=98.0/
---FIRE VENT
&VENT XB=..., SURF_ID='FIRE'/
52
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:63==
Because the AIT value is now larger than ambient temperature, the fuel will not ignite
on its own. A source of ignition needs to be introduced to initiate combustion. In this
case a box with surface temperatures of 900 °C is used to heat up the fuel and initiate
combustion. The box is timed by iterative testing and found to sustain a fast fire if present
for 80 seconds. The inclusion of this box is purely a means of igniting the fuel and should
not impact the growth or output of the fire, it is removed after 80 seconds of heating, with
little impact to the HRR as seen on fig. 6.19 and fig. 6.21.
FDS definition of fuel igniter configured for fast fire
---FUEL IGNITER
&OBST XB=..., DEVC_ID='TIMER',
SURF_IDS='HOT_SURF','HOT_SURF','HOT_SURF' /
&DEVC ID='TIMER', XYZ=..., QUANTITY='TIME',
SETPOINT= 80.0,INITIAL_STATE=.TRUE. /
&SURF ID='HOT_SURF', TMP_FRONT=900.0, TMP_INNER=900.0,
RAMP_T='HotRamp', COLOR='RED' /
&RAMP ID='HotRamp' T= 0.0, F=0.0 /
&RAMP ID='HotRamp' T= 1.0, F=1.0 /
&RAMP ID='HotRamp' T=80.0, F=1.0 /
&RAMP ID='HotRamp' T=81.0, F=0.0 /
The resulting HRR and pressure developments are compared.

bulk, and localised methods use a modification of the HVAC module, the concentration of
the leak must be of influence to the oscillations.
This simple test shows that both methods behave the same way during the pressure
increase, but different when the pressure drop happens. The abrupt drop in HRR is due
to oxygen depletion (more on this later) and this abrupt loss in heat gain creates an under
pressure where new air can be drawn in through the specified leak. When this influx of
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
57
==PAGE:68==
new air happens the two models behave differently. The localised leak draws in air faster
than the bulk leakage due to a re-ignition close to the specified local leakage vent.
(a) Bulk leakage
(b) Local leakage
Figure 6.28: Combustion happening after oxygen depletion due to leaking air entering the
compartment.
The localised leak seems to help increase the HRR, possibly because of the concentrated
area of air entering. For the bulk leakage sporadic combustion is happening at every
surface specified with a leakage. Due to the concentrated burning a spike in is seen for
the local leakage (at 225 seconds) were the pressure follows shortly as the temperature
and thus pressure will increase as a result. The same is seen to a lesser degree for the
bulk leak, here the sporadic burning increases the pressure more gradually.
6.5
CFAST modeling and validation of FDS
Best case for validation of results, would be to have experimental data to compare against.
This is rarely the case with regular work done by engineers in practise, as the problems are
most probably outside the scope of experimental applicability. Another validation method
is to compare against idealised models were one is certain that physical laws are obeyed
and the simpler model can be better defended. These idealised models usually give some
bounds on the parameters ie. maximum or minimum of expected quantities. One such
model concerning fire dynamics is the two (or one) zone model governed by transport
equations applied to mass and energy of a control volume.
The zone model is widely applied by both researchers and engineers because of its very
simple nature. As an engineering approach the zone model is more attractive because of
the simple application and time consumption versus a CFD code such as FDS. The two
zone model excels where defined stratification is present in the gas of the control volume.
The simple zone model is used to gain some form of validity in the more complex re-
sults of FDS. Even though the reviewed literature states that low-ventilated compartment
fires develop more gradual temperate profiles. The comparison is also used to show the
limitations of a simpler model.
Instead of calculating by hand or script, which would take some time away from the thesis
and is somewhat out of scope, the program CFAST is employed. CFAST is similar to FDS
and developed by the same organisation (NIST). CFAST essentially solves the two/single
zone model where the governing equations solves two balances; one between mass,
based on pressure differences; and one between energy, based on balance between
energy released by the fire and energy leaving the compartment.
Because CFAST is a simpler model, some effects like leakage and combustion, are not
as detailed as they are in FDS. In the CFAST model a vent is created at one of the walls
with a area of 0.0055 m2 simulating the leakage defined in FDS with the same area.
58
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:69==

Several parameters are monitored including the CFAST zone temperatures and FDS ap-

A two zone model only captures the temperature in the upper and lower zones where
FDS can capture the temperature at the center of every mesh cell. It is therefore possi-
ble to make continuous measurements in FDS, which usually results in more gradually
changing temperature profiles. To be able to compare FDS against two zone models an
approximation of a two zone model has been implemented as an output quantity in FDS
this is what is compared with CFAST in the following figure.

By comparing the temperatures it is seen that the single zone model better describes the
conditions in the FDS model but the temperatures in the FDS model is significantly higher
than the zone models. The temperatures found in FDS lie somewhat close together imply-
ing a much more uniform distribution of the temperature. Referring back to the literature
study this was also found in some experiments where the smoke layer relatively quickly
filled the entire enclosure. Looking at the smoke layer height fig. 6.32 the difference is
explained more clearly. The smoke layer quite drastically dominates in the FDS simula-
tion, and the stratified assumption is clearly a worse choice compared to the single zone
model in this case.

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:71==
A significant effect that CFAST can not predict is the pyrolysis rate in oxygen starved
environments. CFAST purely reflects the HRR specified by the user, the paralysis rate
is assumed fixed and only the magnitude of the plume energy released is changed by a
empirical relation based on the amount of available oxygen (per CFAST technical guide).
In FDS the pyrolysis rate changes with the availability of oxygen reducing the release of
burned species. The differences between CFAST and FDS results in CFAST predicting
higher pressures in oxygen starved compartments because of the comparatively higher
release rate of combustion species. Part of this effect is seen on fig. 6.33 where the
predicted pressure by CFAST is much higher than found by FDS. This must, as explained,
be partly because of the limitations in CFAST but may also be attributed to the difference
in leakage modelling between the two models.
0
50
100
150
200
250
300
Time [s]
-10000
-5000
0
5000
10000
15000
Pressure
[Pa]
FDS Pressure
Two zone LOC=15%
Two zone LOC=13%
Single zone LOC=15%
Single zone LOC=13%
Figure 6.33: Pressure development comparison between CFAST and FDS.
CFAST quite accurately represents the HRR and especially the point of oxygen depletion.
A question then arises, ”why not use CFAST instead of FDS?”
Although benefits of using a simpler model are large in terms of time spent simulating.
FDS can handle many more effects of a fire, in addition to also being able to simulate a
HVAC network. Another reason not to choose CFAST in this particular case with low leak-
age compartment fires, is that the literature study showed that the two zone model may
not be applicable to these kinds of scenarios. A defined upper and lower zone or single
zone may not develop in these cases and this is a problem for CFAST which assumes
either strong stratification or uniform zone parameters. The discussion made on the differ-
ences between FDS and CFAST should not be taken as definitive answers. For a better
comparison the FDS model could be configured to use simpler models, but this some-
what goes against the use of FDS in the first place. All in all FDS can predict the fire more
accurately while simultaneously taking into consideration several more effects. From the
results in this test CFAST is seen to predict the HRR relatively accurately. An engineer
should be able to use CFAST for a quick estimate of the point of oxygen depletion.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
61
==PAGE:72==
6.6
Case 1: Concluding remarks
Stability keywords
A short description of the most relevant HVAC and pressure solver settings are found.
Supplemented by a short parameter study on the HVAC_PRES_RELAX, HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE
is carried out showing the effect of including either one or both. The pressure solver it-
erations are examined by including the VELOCITY_ERROR_FILE on the DUMP line. When
simulating several compartments the amount of iterations by the pressure solver can be
lowered substantially by not having the meshes touch boundaries.
Mesh analysis
During the mesh analysis it became apparent that the majority of the main outputs like
HRR, pressure, and duct velocities differ only very slightly. A difference is seen in the
timing of the rapid drop of HRR. For the 0.1 and 0.05 meter grids the time to the drop
is longer than the 0.2 grid. The fluctuations or oscillations of the HRR is seen to be
slightly dependant on the grid size. The fine mesh of 0.05 grid show larger (not extremely)
oscillations than the other grids, especially for the duct velocities. It is explained by the
combustion’s dependence on the fuel to air ratio, which can be more accurately estimated
by smaller grid volumes. Meshing of multiple rooms is explored by running simulations
of two different grid sizes for the connected room. For efficient use of FDS, all meshes
should have equal gridding. A coarse mesh would result in wasted CPU time as it would
sit idle and wait for the finer mesh to be resolved.
Design fire
With the findings of the previous analyses the medium, fast, and ultra-fast t2 definitions are
tested. For all fire growths the MAX HRR of 4000 kW is never reached. At some point the
level of oxygen concentration causes a drop in the HRR and thereby the pressure. The
shapes of the different pressure developments are seen to correlate well with literature.
Extinction
Three different behaviours of the fire is portrayed in literature. In order to try and get
a more precise solution different values of the AIT parameter was tested. In this case
the rapid extinction behaviour seemed most appropriate. All tests eventually showed
complete extinction of the fire. Some of the higher value ignition temperatures resulted in
back-draft like phenomena where a lot of air would enter the compartment before it was
ignited. A design fire modelling the rapid extinction was made using a script that could
find the limiting oxygen concentration. The rapid extinction design fire correlates well with
the AIT test.
Leakage
In literature it has been seen that leakage of the envelope has large impact on the pres-
sure peak. FDS operates between two models of leakage, both are tested. No significant
difference is seen for either HRR or pressure until after the HRR drop. The Bulk leakage
method introduces new air slower than the localised leakage method. A larger concen-
tration of air enters at the localised leakage compared to over the entire envelope surface
for the bulk leakage method.
CFAST model
A CFAST simulation is conducted to show the limitations and validate the base FDS cal-
culations. HRR and oxygen concentration are well represented, as well as the drop in
HRR or point of oxygen depletion. The temperature is higher in the FDS model compared
to CFAST and a single zone model is seen to better predict the conditions of the room. A
large difference is seen for the pressure development, the simpler combustion model of
CFAST is believed to be the main cause.
62
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:73==
7
Parameter study: Case 2
A two story building with five compartments connected on each floor. A vertical main duct
connects the two floors. The case will be used to study different configurations of the
HVAC network. The different configurations should show the current method of mitigating
smoke spread via HVAC networks. The case is simulated with realistic fans and pres-
sure losses (with the assumptions made in the previous case) in order to capture realistic
figures of the different configurations.
Criteria for success is also introduced by monitoring smoke spread to the connected
rooms. Any smoke spread will result in a failure since the danish standard requires no
spread of smoke between rooms. The results of the tests are shown qualitatively by look-
ing at the soot density on smokeview, supplemented with figures of the flow in the relevant
ducts.

in the room strongly dictates the point of oxygen depletion. The fire implemented is the
worst fast growing fire that can be present in a closed compartment with this geometry.

The FDS job is run on the HPC cluster by asking for 10 MPI processes split 5 per node.
Each MPI process is threaded by using 4 cores on the node. 5 · 4 = 20, one node has 20
cores, meaning the entire job will use two nodes. See the following batch script snippet
#BSUB -n 10
#BSUB -R "span[ptile=5] affinity[core(4)]"

7.1
Case 2: Test 0 - Nominal flow test
The size of the HVAC network is relatively large compared to the previous tests. As
suggested in the FDS theory and application section, a test is performed to verify the
nominal flow conditions in the network. Five rooms that all must be supplied by 0.05 m3/s
of air gives 0.25 m3/s for one floor and 0.5 m3/s for both floors.
The implemented fan is modelled by a quadratic fan curve tuned to give the correct volume
flow of 0.5 m3/s at a pressure loss of roughly 50 Pa.
The performance is tested without fire and the following figures verify that the fan imple-
mented can satisfy the supply requirements and the model accurately depicts the intended
flow. In the main ducts the volume flow should start from duct JH with a value of 0.25 m3/s
and fall by 0.05 m3/s for each tee. The HVAC solver is seen to find a steady solution at
around 5 seconds fig. 7.4.

Figure 7.4: Volume flow in supply ducts of floor 0.
The stable value for the supply vent ducts are not precisely 0.05 m3/s. In a real HVAC
system, dampers would be installed in each branch duct and adjusted to balance the flow.
For example, the damper in duct HI0 and FG0 would be closed slightly to decrease the
flow, this would in turn increase the supplied flow to ducts, DE0, BC0, and BA0. The dif-
ference seen in fig. 7.4b is not large considering the relative accuracy when dimensioning
HVAC networks. It is assumed that possible effects of not fine tuning the system would
be negligible.
An entire separate study could go into detail of the impact of hot gasses and smoke trans-
port in ducts. FDS can not model heat loss or damage to the ducts and should in this
case be considered as transporting of mass only.
The pressure that a fan must deliver is given by the largest total pressure drop in the
system. In this case where the tee losses are neglected the largest pressure loss is found
at the point longest from the fan (ie. nodes connected to room 01).
66
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:77==
A quick hand calculation of the pressure loss from room 01 to the fan assuming a friction
loss of 0.5 Pa/m (good estimate acc. [31]) gives a pressure loss of 47 Pa. Note the differ-
ence in height has minuscule effect as the temperature is constant and the atmospheric
presssure change is very small.
∆pvent,A = 30 Pa
∆pfriction = 0.5 Pa/m · 34.5 m = 17.25 Pa
∆p = pvent,A + pfriction = 47.25 Pa
The fan pressure found by the HVAC solver is slightly larger than the estimate, this is
due to the assumption of a constant loss of 0.5 Pa/m. In the actual simulation the friction
loss is calculated dynamically and the loss may be slightly larger than the assumed value.
Again the value stabilises at around 5 seconds.

In reality the loss would be larger because of other components in the network. The
HVAC unit especially contributes to the pressure loss as it usually consists of filters and
other components that hinder the flow. A rough estimate of the pressure loss of an HVAC
unit is given in [31] were a variable air volume unit that has to deliver 0.5 m3/s has an
approximate loss of 200 Pa.

7.2
Case 2: Test 1 - HVAC on
In this test the default performance of the HVAC network will be monitored. A more realistic
fan curve is implemented along with a loss of 200 Pa over the fan to simulate the HVAC
unit components as described in the earlier section. Realistic vent fittings are also found
to show that the implemented 30 Pa pressure loss is realistic appendix G.
The needed volume flow is as specified above eaual to 0.5 m3/s or 1800 m3/h.
A
BESB315-4-1FC ventilator unit from Exhausto1 is implemented as supply and exhaust
fan. It has its optimal operating range at a flow of 1930 m3/h at a total driving pressure of
371 Pa appendix G.
The quadratic fan curve is defined by a max pressure and max volume flow. The maximum
pressure defines the point where the volume flow is 0 and the maximum volume flow
defines the point of 0 pressure. The fan flow that FDS employs is found at the point
where the HVAC solver find equilibrium between the pressure loss (∆p) in the system
and the flow at that point on the fan curve. The quadratic fan curve is defined by the
following relation [28]:
˙V (∆p) = ˙Vmax · sign(∆Pmax −∆p)
√
|∆p −∆Pmax|
∆Pmax
(7.1)
Using a max pressure of 600 Pa and a max flow of 1000 L/s (or 1.0 m3/s) a reasonable
approximation to the box ventilator fan curve can be seen on fig. 7.6. This is not the same
fan curve used in the HVAC test(0) because now the fan is working to its full capacity and
not limited in power as in the previous test.

Figure 7.6: Quadratic fan curve compared with box ventilator BESB315 appendix G
The quadratic curve can go outside of the limits of the actual ventilator. It is assumed that
this is an appropriate assumption as volume flows above the max flow would result in a
negative pressure difference acting as a hindrance rather than a drive of the flow. Just
like a stationary fan would hinder the flow like a damper.
1Industry developer and manufacturer of HVAC units and components
68
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:79==
In reality the outer limits of a ventilator fan are not defined and the designer would have
to make assumptions on the characteristics outside of the technical information, as is
done here. It is possible that some ventilator fans would not be able to operate outside
of its limits. Temperature and air density will have an impact en the fan curve but the
impact of high or very high temperatures are not tested or documented by manufactures.
The BESB315 ventilator box is functional at temperatures up to 80 °C according to the
manufacturer.
7.2.1
Test 1: Results
The results of test 1 should show smoke spread through the HVAC network as the supply
dampers are open. Contrary to standard practise were dampers are closed in the supply
network when the temperature sensors detect temperatures of 72 °C2. The primary results
to consider first is the output of the fire and the pressure development in the compartment.
The results can be seen in the following figures where the output is averaged over 9
seconds.
From FDS the print interval is user configured to output every 1 seconds, but by averaging
over 9 seconds the oscillations are ”removed”. The oscillations are still a result of com-
bustion close to the vents and the instantaneous transport of flow from the HVAC solver
at each time step. The physical location of the fire has not been verified after oxygen
depletion, as described in the previous sections.

captures this point where slight smoke spread is also observed at the supply vents on floor
1. This smoke is quickly dissipated and disappears from visibility shortly after the peak.
The HVAC solver model is clearly seen in this figure as the node model instantaneously
at 135 seconds, shows smoke spread in all of the rooms on floor 1.
The reversed flow is ongoing for roughly 140 seconds until the point of oxygen depletion
that can be observed on fig. 7.11. In this test the stay-in-place practise would not be
appropriate.

7.3
Case 2: Test 2 - HVAC off
Because the reliability is of interest to designers a scenario where the HVAC fans are
not running can be used as a bounding scenario. This configuration in fact simulates a
scenario where the dampers are malfunctioning (not closing) and both fans are stopped.
The fans are not removed but simulated by a pressure loss, or so called ”flue gas resis-
tance”. The loss is given by the manufacturer and acts like a vent loss appendix G. The
loss is implemented instead of the fan in the FDS input file.
7.3.1
Test 2: Results
The primary results are shown below for the fire compartment. From the perspective of
the HVAC network the driving force is now the fire in the compartment. Smoke spread
seems inevitable as the fire is now essentially acting as a fan.

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
73
==PAGE:84==
Figure 7.16: Soot density at 183 seconds (smoke spreads to floor 1).
Figure 7.17: Soot density at 210 seconds (point of oxygen depletion).
No smoke spread is visible before the pressure peak. After this point the HRR and as a
consequence the pressure start to fluctuate because of the decreasing level of oxygen.
This causes the smoke to travel ”backwards and forwards” at the vents (no mass is stored
in the HVAC system as per the solver model). The fluctuations causes some smoke to
enter the other compartments.
Only very slight smoke spread is observed at the peak pressure seen on fig. 7.15 where
the maximum duct flow is also seen.
The reason for the relatively low smoke spread compared to test 1 is because the pressure
loss at the vents are larger than the loss over the stationary fan. The flow follows the path
of least resistance which in this case is directly to the ambient through the main vertical
duct. This shows that the presumed safety of vents are to a large extent okay so far as
the fire is not causing large oscillations in the pressure. Vents installed in Denmark must,
as discussed in the regulations section, have a pressure loss of at least 30 Pa (at nominal
flow) and sustain this loss at a temperature of 400 °C.

mirrored about the y-axis. This is due to the flow direction definition in the input file where
flows opposite to the duct naming is considered negative (ie. flow from node A to node B
is negative).
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure

The fluctuating nature is clearly visualised in fig. 7.19 a and b. Smoke spread is present
despite the very small velocities in the ducts. To validate these velocities the claim re-
garding the flow using the path of least resistance is further explained by looking at the
stationary fan at low volume flows. See fig. 7.20 redrawn after the technical data of the
box ventilator and the vent armature from the manufacturer.

It is clearly visible that the loss is much grater at the vents, and this is seen in the simulation

has very limited resources a simple comparison between the pressure losses can give
some insight into the reliability of the system preventing smoke spread. The practise of
comparing pressure differences is comparable to the method used in the danish norm [1]
concerning the prescriptive solutions of HVAC systems.

==PAGE:87==
7.4
Case 2: Test 3 - Standard practise
Dampers are installed in the branch ducts connected to the supply vents and the supply
fan is shut off. The test now in some ways simulates a building with an exhaust system
only. A setup with only an exhaust system would in reality be accompanied by some sort
of fresh air intake [31]. The under pressure created by the exhaust system at normal
operation would draw in fresh air by this passive intake. The presence of such intake
would in this test result in a lower pressure and possibly lower risk of smoke spread. In
this test a fresh air intake is not modelled because an intake was not present to begin
with.
The exhaust fan is running with the same max pressure and flow as before. The expected
results are a higher pressure peak and larger duct flow velocities. Based on the reduced
area of pressure relief from the supply ducts that have now been closed by dampers.

fig. 7.23b the two previous tests are compared against the current. It is seen that the
pressure development of test 3 first drops because of the negative pressure created by
the exhaust fan. The pressure from the fire counteracts this negative pressure and quickly
overpowers the fan (at least in the fire compartment).

decrease in HRR, a larger difference between room temperature and ambient temper-

other tests.

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:89==
Smoke spread is observed qualitatively again by looking at the soot density in smokeview.
Figure 7.25: Soot density at 126 seconds (pressure peak).
Figure 7.26: Soot density at 188 seconds (smoke has been removed by exhaust fan).
Figure 7.27: Soot density at 210 seconds (point of oxygen depletion).
The intended outcome of this test was to show if the standard practise of closing the supply
network helps prevent the spread of smoke. In this test, even with the large increase in
pressure, only comparatively little smoke spread is seen.
It is of interest to point out that smoke did spread to all rooms on both floors at the pressure
peak seen on fig. 7.25, but the exhaust fan gradually removed this smoke again as seen in
fig. 7.26, where smoke can only be observed in the room closest to the fire compartment.
Just before the point of oxygen depletion the fluctuating nature of the oxygen starved fire
results in smoke spread throughout the system. Although to a much smaller degree than
seen in the previous tests because of the under pressure created by the exhaust fan.
In this test the temperatures found in the ducts would inhibit or damage the fan unit as
temperatures are substantially higher than the verified operational temperature of 80 °C.
This is assuming no loss of heat through the duct walls and connections which would
happen in reality.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
79
==PAGE:90==
7.5
Case 2: Concluding remarks
Test 0
As demonstrated in the estimation of the pressure loss is is seen that the HVAC solver
accurately models a given ventilation network at nominal conditions. Limitations in terms
of the HVAC solver is not seen in cases with no fire present.
Test 1
Running the HVAC network with no changes is demonstrated as bad practise. A counter
acting fan, in this case the supply fan, creates very defined reversed volume flows in
the main ducts of the HVAC system. Reversed flow was seen for the majority of the time
during the pressure build up. Smoke is seen primarily spreading through the supply vents,
as demonstrated by the soot density renderings. The exhaust system does not contribute
to smoke spread before the drop in HRR. At the rapid drop in HRR and pressure the flow
is reversed in the exhaust ducts and any smoke present in the main ducts is suddenly
rushed backwards in the system and into the compartments.
The vertical compartment temperatures lie close together implying a uniform distribution.
Most of the temperature profile shows temperatures between 100-300 °C. Investigating
the temperature in the ducts it is seen that roughly 20 seconds of smoke spread would be
possible before activation of any dampers that might be present.
Test 2
Shutting the HVAC fans off and passively exhausting the smoke seems a worse case in
terms of spread to multiple floors. In terms of overall smoke spread, a total shut down
of the HVAC system seems to work better than leaving the fans running. Investigating
the flows in the ducts reveal that the pressure loss at the vents have large impact on the
behaviour. By shutting down the fans, a path of least resistance is created where for the
smoke moves with the help of the pressure build-up and buoyancy, past the branch ducts
creating only small flows in these. However, the small fluctuating flows cause a problem.
”Puffs” of smoke are seen during the limited availability of oxygen causing the fluctuations
in the ducts to spread smoke.
Temperatures are slightly higher than test 1.
Test 3
A standard practise procedure is tested. Not being the one and only standard solution,
but a configuration that could be thought of as simple and in accordance with normal
practise. Installing dampers in every supply branch and closing them from the start of the
simulation. Because of the decrease in effective ”leakage” area or relief of the pressure
from the HVAC network the pressure increase is larger than previous tests.
The exhaust fan is seen to create a substantial negative pressure before and after the
pressure peak is seen. The HVAC solver is perfectly air tight and the fan is operating to
the conditions presented. This means that for a compartment of low leakage the fan will
run at maximum capacity because it sees a large pressure hindrance which is in fact the
room it self. The negative pressure at the start and end of the simulation is roughly -200
Pa, which is quite large and may be dangerous to any occupants. If the doors open in such
a way that the negative pressure inhibits opening of the doors, which is the same effect as
seen with over pressure in the fire compartment, but now the problem is spread to every
room. In real systems a damper would be installed just before the smoke fan, which is
controlled by a pressure sensor, such that too large under pressures will be mitigated.
Temperatures are higher than both test 1 and 2, especially at the upper layers.
80
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:91==
8
Parameter study: Case 3
8.1
Dedicated smoke ventilation configuration
In this case the previous tests are used to study the implications of a more realistic config-
uration of the HVAC system. The geometry of the compartments and the HVAC system
is exactly the same as case 2 but now is now fitted with a dedicated smoke ventilator.
It is common practise to have a separate smoke ventilator as part of an HVAC system
in multistory buildings or buildings with centralised HVAC solutions. This is due to the
relatively large pressure loss (as described above) over the HVAC unit. Another reason
is the damage to components. In the event of a fire where only one compartment has
burned, the damage to the HVAC unit, filters, etc. can be large and costly to repair. A
separate smoke ventilator can be coupled onto both the supply and exhaust network, cir-
cumventing the entire HVAC unit and the major pressure loss thus easing the workload
on the smoke ventilator.

Smoke Fan

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
81
==PAGE:92==
In a scenario like this the smoke ventilator is not used to keep tenable conditions in the fire
compartment. It is used to prevent smoke spread from the fire to other compartments. A
smoke fan should be chosen on the basis of ability to keep a negative pressure in all of the
compartments (omitting the fire compartment) at the pressure peak. In real systems with
dedicated smoke ventilators a damper would be installed before the fan to dynamically
change the flow such that a pressure difference of max 100 Pa is present in the network.
This is to prevent occupants from not being able to open the doors. In the danish code
[1] a system like this one is explained and the occupants may not experience pressures
that result in 100 Newtons of force on doors. It is in fact the same exact problematic (only
under pressure) found for the over-pressure created by the fire in the fire compartment.
100 Newtons translate roughly into 10 kg of pulling on the door handle.
The parameters investigated in this case are the influence of the ventilator fan and vent
loss on the default fire and the RAMP fire investigated in the design fire test. By default
fire meaning a HRR defined by a growth phase and a constant max HRR. The RAMP
fire will be defined by the same procedure used in the design fire tests, where a RAMP
is created to drop the HRR to 0 at the point of oxygen depletion. The fan curves are
modelled similar to case 2 with a quadratic relation between flow and pressure. A unit
from the same manufacturer is used to show a realistic max pressure and volume flow for
the FDS input.

8.2.2
Test C3_FC150 qualitative remarks on the fire
The shape of fire plume is similar for all tests up until just before POD. At this point the
Flame is still connected to the designated fire surface. Shortly after the POD the flame
erratically moves away from the fire surface, moving to where ever the oxygen concen-
tration allows combustion. Just before 250 seconds the erratic flames moves towards the
vents. At roughly 250-260 seconds combustion originates from the vents with an oscillat-
ing behaviour. from 250-260 seconds until the end of the simulation combustion slowly
shifts from originating purely from the vents, to only burning at the surfaces of the leaking
walls.
At the pressure peak in the fire compartment very slight smoke spread is seen for test
C3_FC150_PL10 and C3_FC150_PL30, and C3_FC150_PL50 in room 02 (zone 2).
All tests show smoke spread in all rooms on both floors appendix F, and appendix F,
appendix F. The worst FC1500 test in terms of smoke spread overall is C3_FC150_PL10.

==PAGE:103==
Looking again at the equation governing pressure in the ducts and nodes eq. (8.1), the
highlighted term demonstrates the inclusion of buoyancy.
ρjLj
duj
dt = (pi −pk) + (ρg∆z)j + ∆pj −1
2Kjρj|uj|uj
[Pa]
(8.1)
Assuming the fire creates temperatures of roughly 300 degrees in the HVAC network, the
pressure difference between vent node A1 (fire compartment) and ambient node T3 (see
fig. 8.1) is estimated below.
At ambient air has an approximate density of 1.2 kg/m3. At 300 degrees the density of
the hot smoke/air is estimated by use of the ideal gas law:
ρgas =
353.25
300 + 273 = 0.62 kg/m3
(8.2)
If the vents of floor 0 are placed 2 meters above the ground and the main exhaust is placed
at 10 meters above ground the ∆z is 2 m and 8 m respectively.
pA1 = 0.62 · 9.82 · 2 = 12 Pa
pT3 = 1.2 · 9.82 · 10 = 60 Pa
∆pbuoyancy = pT3 −pA1 = 48 Pa
If the vents of floor 1 are placed 5 meters above the ground and the main exhaust is placed
at 2 meters below ground the ∆z is 5 m and -2 m respectively.
pA1 = 0.62 · 9.82 · 5 = 30 Pa
pT3 = 1.2 · 9.82 · (−2) = −12 Pa
∆pbuoyancy = pT3 −pA1 = −42 Pa
The pressure difference should in fact be taken at floor 1 because the pressure difference
at the nodes of floor 1 is higher than at floor 0. For the FC270 fan the 42 Pa is a pressure
that must be overcome by the fan in addition to the pressure generated by the fire.
This is not the exact same difference that FDS would find as it accounts not only for the
two nodes considered but the entire system of nodes and also the temperature might differ
from the estimated 300 °C. The general theory behind the HVAC solution however, show
that a fan might have to overcome a relatively large pressure generated by the effects of
buoyancy. 42 Pa accounts for 15% of the capacity of the FC270 fan.

==PAGE:105==
8.7
Case 3: Discussions
The heat release rate, time to pressure peak, and point of oxygen depletion are all similar
for all tests. The difference is almost negligible. The similar HRR are easily explained by
the fact that it is a pre-defined curve (fast growing fire) and the point of oxygen depletion
happens at similar times. It is believed that the POD is first of all dependant on the amount
of initial oxygen in the room, then later on the supplied amount. With a regular ventilation
rate, the volume of the room plays a larger role in the size of the fire in domestic housing
(0.05-0.1 m3/s). This has become apparent in all three cases, where similar HRR peaks
and POD’s have been observed. The reason for similar time to pressure peak is a bit
more obscure, one would think that a lower vent loss or an increase in fan capacity could
allow for a larger volume flow thus making the time to pressure peak longer. The results
show that the dimensions of the ducts probably play a larger role in pressure relief than
other parameters as the different fan curves did not have much effect on the pressure.
At the pressure peak where the smoke spread is assumed to be most severe, all of the
results showed no or very little smoke spread (observe appendix F). A designer might use
the results found at pressure peak to determine if smoke spread will occur.
The setup with dedicated smoke fan somewhat looks like a cross between ”case 2 test 2
- HVAC off” and ”case 2 test 3 - exhaust only”. With both networks working as exhausts
the loss over the vents are crucial in understanding the way the air is moving in the ducts.
The flow prioritises the path of least resistance as stated in case 2. The flow runs mostly
past the branch ducts connected to the compartments creating very small volume flows.
Smoke from the main duct flows instantly to the branching ducts into the connected com-
partment.
After the peak pressure all simulations showed smoke spread to some degree. The den-
sity of smoke/soot is larger for the weaker fan compared to the stronger. The same trend
is seen for the vent loss, where a larger loss leads to less smoke spread.
Smoke fan running from beginning of fire, in reality the fan would initiate as soon as a
temperature of 72 degrees is observed in the network. From earlier tests this was seen to
be around 80 seconds. The impact of including the fan from the start may have an effect
on the key numbers presented.
Using a setup where smoke is only exhausted and not also supplied, or without any pres-
sure relief in the envelope, the negative pressure can become quite large in some of the
tests. On the fan pressure figures fig. 8.5-fig. 8.14 it can be seen that the fan tries to keep
under pressure in all of the rooms. Because it is exhaust only the leaking is dominant
at times where the pressure is not increasing. on fig. 8.5 and fig. 8.8 the limit of the fan
is reached as the pressure builds. For the other tests the fan capacity is never reached
indicating that the fan can handle the over pressure created by the fire.
Because the vent soffit is placed 0.5 meters below the ceiling a smoke layer can form
below the ceiling. On many of the smoke renderings in the appendix F this layer can
clearly be seen showing soot density before the point of oxygen depletion. After the point
of oxygen depletion the smoke spreads further because of the large pressure drop and
the following oscillations.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
95
==PAGE:106==
At the point of oxygen depletion, smoke has spread to all rooms in all tests, even with the
pressure not being at its peak. The fluctuations created by the oxygen starved flame is
believed to cause small oscillations in the ducts of the connected rooms. These fluctua-
tions push and pull on air from the main duct which already has smoke present from the
pressure build up phase.

Figure 8.16: Possible explanation for smoke spread despite negative zone pressure.
The large over pressure can, as stated in the introduction, prevent occupants from opening
inwards facing doors. A pressure of 100 Pa is suggested in both danish regulations and
in [3] as max. This is because a pressure of 100 Pa on a door (roughly 2 m2) creates a
force of 200 Newtons or roughly 20 kg on the door handle. If the occupant is disabled or
elderly, 20 kg can be inhibiting. Pressures of 100 Pa and above are present in all of the
simulations.
96
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:107==
The HVAC solver cannot store mass [30]. The solution procedure thus instantly transfers
mass from one end to another. This instant transfer of mass is believed to increase the
fluctuating nature of the solution. A small indication of this can be observed by looking at
the flow in the branch ducts. See the figure below showing the volume flow in the branch
duct connecting the main supply to zone 2.

vent loss the change in pressure peak becomes less and less.
