==PAGE:109==
Continuing explanation of table 8.14: With the smallest vent loss (PL10) the standard
deviation is relatively significant showing that the fan curve has some influence on the
peak pressure. With increasing vent loss the fan curve has less impact on the peak
pressure.
One must remember that the loss is placed at the vent and the dynamics may change if
the loss was moved somewhere else in the system. The reason for applying the loss at
the vent was to find if it changed the way the fan curve interacted with the system.
Having the loss only at the vents is an easy way to dimension the flow correctly. Having
equal losses at every vent means that it is the dimension of the ducts that governs the
volume flow because the fan tries to balance its output with the largest loss in the system.
Assuming that the pressure is correctly predicted and that the smoke spread after the
pressure peak is caused by limitations of the HVAC solver the following could be said:
If a designer were to pick one of the tested fan curves the choice would be between FC270
and FC400. FC270 slightly under performs and allows for over pressure in zone 2. The
FC270 tests was right on the edge of keeping the rooms in negative pressure difference.
FC400 never reached the capacity of the fan with -64 Pa as max in zone 2, which is
acceptable. FC400 show that with dampers placed in the system the fan could be kept
at a pressure difference of max 100 Pa and still not reach its capacity. This means that
a damper must be installed because the fan else would create high under pressures that
could hinder opening of doors.
It has been shown that the vertical location of the fan does not have large influence on
the pressure peak. However, the impact on the fan is of relevance as seen in table 8.12
and table 8.13. Comparing the tests with the fan located above and below ground (see
appendix C), shows that the difference is roughly equal to the estimated value of 40 Pa
extra placed on the fan as a result of moving it 2 meters below ground. For the FC270 fan
curve the change in fan location causes massive smoke spread to both floors with smoke
starting at floor 1. The smoke spreading first to floor 1 is an indication of the fan not being
able to counteract the buoyant force of the smoke.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
99
==PAGE:110==
100
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:111==
9
Guidelines
A designer should always configure input to fit any given case as closely as possible.
However due to time constraints or other limitations a full study is rarely carried out. From
the investigations done in the literature study, application of FDS, and the parameter study
the following list of points, should be considered when doing simulations similar in nature
like the ones seen in the thesis.
- Not much data is available on the matter, but the available experiments and simula-
tions studies show that pressure peaks over 100 Pa are seen for most fires, even at
normal leakage rates. Validation studies of FDS [17] etc. show that the conditions
of the fire in low-ventilated, low-leakage type apartments can be well predicted by
FDS.
- Pressure differences over 100 Pa can result in difficulties of opening doors.
- Full scale studies studies on compartments of roughly 50 m2 or smaller.
- The fire growth rate has a large impact on the pressure peak.
- Consider the leakage class carefully, a change in envelope leakage has large impact
on the pressure peak. Almost exponential in nature acc. [21].
- The bulk leakage method better describes a leakage if the actual sources of leakage
is not known. If the leakage path is known the localised leakage will perform better.
- Know that FDS can model a dynamic leakage coefficient, but available data on
dynamic leakage coefficients is almost non-existent.
- Introducing all of the pressure losses in an HVAC system can quickly become an
exhaustive task.
- Begin by implementing the largest losses and losses at connections between ambi-
ent and FDS domain.
- Tee’s can be implemented without accounting for the losses but only if the diameters
of the connected ducts are correctly sized and the ratio between them is not to large
or small (ie. large difference in relative flow velocity.)
- The friction loss can beneficially be calculated by FDS, only a value of the duct
material roughness is needed.
- Know that the HVAC solver behaves better with larger pressure losses at the con-
nection between FDS and the HVAC nodes.
- Very small loss coefficients can lead to the HVAC solver using longer to find a stable
solution. The HVAC solver solves a set of differential equations that if no losses are
defined become unstable.
- The ventilator fans can be modelled by either a quadratic fan curve or a user defined
ramp. The former is simpler in terms of implementation, and can easily be fitted to
technical data.
- Know that when implementing the manufacturer fan curve, one cannot be sure that
this is the correct behaviour under fire.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
101
==PAGE:112==
- Three combustion behaviours are seen for under ventilated compartment fires.
1 Steady burning, seen with higher oxygen supply rates, open doors and win-
dows etc. may develop into flashover fire if enough oxygen is supplied
2 Low frequency oscillating behaviour, oscillations with periods of 50-100 sec-
onds. Behaviour found between steady burning and rapid extinction. Hard to
replicate in FDS with a simple combustion model.
3 Rapid extinction; fire extinguishes it self as all of the oxygen present in the room
is used (down to the limiting oxygen concentration).
- Implement the relaxation keywords HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE and HVAC_PRES_RELAX.
1 Set HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE to TRUE.
2 Set HVAC_PRES_RELAX to a value larger than 0.5, possibly closer to 1.0 than 0.5.
- Know that the resolution of the grid impacts the combustion model and slightly in-
creases oscillating behaviour.
- Literature and this thesis found that a grid of 0.1 meter cells resolves the HRR, and
pressure sufficiently.
- With the simple combustion model the high frequency oscillations seen in this study
is not validated.
- Try to either limit the ghosting flames or make sure that the combustion after oxygen
depletion is physically correct by looking at the temperature compared with the AIT.
- Know that the AIT (Auto Ignition Temperature) can not be predicted for the sim-
ple combustion model. The actual ignition temperature of the fuel depends on the
conditions of the individual cell, size, and fual to air mixture.
- Using FDS specifically, one should consider the code snippets seen throughout the
thesis. All of the snippets are collected in appendix A in typical FDS input file layout
order.
102
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:113==
10
Conclusion
The aim of this master thesis was to develop further understanding of the under ventilated
compartment fire scenario. Especially concerning the large pressure developments that
have been observed in literature. A literature study and a parameter study explores the
matter. The literature study summarises reports and articles ranging from 1980 to 2019.
The parameter study is carried out using three cases of increasing complexity.
The main hypothesis of the thesis was: ”Smoke spread is possible between rooms con-
nected with mechanical ventilation, because of large over-pressures in the beginning of
a compartment fire.”
The short conclusion to the hypothesis regarding the findings of this thesis is:
Using a simple combustion model, FDS allows for burning wherever the fuel to air ratio
allows it. This creates problems of continued burning where oxygen can enter. Burning
at vents create a fluctuating behaviour in the HRR and pressure of the compartment. The
validity of these oscillations are yet to be determined as physical phenomena or numerical
in nature. During the growth phase of the fire a significant pressure build-up can occur.
Configuring the HVAC with a dedicated smoke fan ensures that only slight smoke spread
is seen at the pressure peak. When the fire is starved of oxygen it begins to fluctuate,
the fluctuations result in smoke spread in all tested cases. A designer can thus not only
consider the peak pressure but should also consider the transient behaviour of the fire,
especially during the oxygen limited phase.
With the hypothesis some followup questions were asked and the findings of these are
summarised below:
When dimensioning an HVAC system in terms of fire and smoke spread the danish stan-
dard DS428 allows for use of prescribed- and performance-based solutions. The pre-
scribed solutions use predefined input to show appropriate applications. These predefined
systems are dimensioned after vent losses, stack effects, a compartment temperature of
400 °C, and a 200 Pa pressure in the fire compartment. The temperature correlates well
with the results found in the parameter study. The danish standard DS428 does describe
how pressures of 2000 Pa are seen in closed low-leakage compartments. In the param-
eter study it is shown that a system with a leakage rate of 1.5 h−1 and a fast fire growth,
shows pressure peaks ranging from 400 to 2000 Pa depending on the HVAC system loss.
The methods applied in standards considers mainly the steady state solution found for
worst case scenarios when the pressure is at its peak. This method omits the transient
behaviour of the fire during the under-ventilated phase. In all of the cases fluctuations
in the HRR is seen. The fluctuations start shortly after the pressure peak because of a
low oxygen concentration. During the pressure increase, smoke is only spreading for the
cases with low vent losses. But all cases show smoke spread after the peak pressure
because of the fluctuations.
The transient behaviour of the fire is of major significance to how the ventilation system
behaves. Three behaviours are seen in literature, steady burning, oscillatory burning, and
rapid extinction. These are transient characteristics of the fire and a simple design fire
cannot capture these behaviours.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
103
==PAGE:114==
From literature and video evidence the impact of large pressure in the early phases of
a compartment fire can be observed. Inwards opening doors should be considers as a
real danger to the safety occupants. Even small fire can result in pressure over 100 Pa.
Using a fast growing fire the smoke layer quickly envelops the entire compartment and
escape can be prevented for approximately 2 minutes during the pressure buildup. It is
very hard to quantify the frequency of incidents where occupants have been prevented
from opening doors or if smoke spread has spread though a ventilation network.
The simple t2 design fire is used to show how an engineer would deal with the simple
combustion model in FDS. The low-frequency oscillations seen in the parameter studies
show that the under-ventilated fire is difficult to model with the simple model. Certain
relaxation parameters and solver configurations can help alleviate the size of the fluctua-
tions but not completely mitigate them. The fluctuations are believed to be connected with
the instantaneous transfer of mass in the HVAC model. Larger pressure losses are seen
to ”dampen” the amplitude of the fluctuations. The validity of continued burning contrary
to rapid extinction should be investigated further.
10.1
Further studies
HRR seems to follow the designed curve up until the point of oxygen depletion. From
the investigations its seems that this point could be predicted by knowing only the growth
rate of the fire and the geometry of the compartment. A study could go into the prediction
of this point by monitoring the immediately available oxygen in the fire compartment. In
addition to monitoring the concentration of oxygen it could also prove beneficial look at if
the sharp drop in HRR caused by the lack of oxygen exists for smaller max HRR, as is
seen for the medium growth fire. A study like this could be used to develop design fires
specialised for low ventilated compartments.
Implementing large scale HVAC networks into FDS is a huge task, taking up much of
model setup time. Several software solutions exits that help HVAC engineers dimen-
sioning large networks. The node model in FDS is very simple and conversion from the
popular software should be doable. Not only should the geometry be easier to implement
but also the parameters of the duct system, dimensions, losses, etc.
Since it is seen that implementing tees is one of the harder part of modeling the HVAC net-
work an experimental or CFD study could be conducted into the validity of pressure losses
even at high temperatures. Investigating impact of reversing flows mainly in branching tee
fittings. Investigate impact of leakage and heat loss in HVAC ducts on the flow character-
istics.
This thesis only implements a very simple centralised HVAC unit, the development of the
construction industry seems to also move towards decentralised systems. These are also
covered in the danish DS428 standard. Decentralised systems are more complex, setup
wise, than centralised solutions. Their function is the same and the risk of smoke spread
should also be verified under the early high pressure phase.
104
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:115==
Moving the fire compartment, it is assumed on a basis of largest pressure loss that the
furthest room is the most critical room. It would be interesting to show if this is actually
the case.
The model in these simulations have the following issues which could be improved or
investigated in another study:
- Geometry issues, smoke rises to the ceiling because of buoyancy, the vents are
placed below the ceiling on the wall. The smoke would first be exhausted when it
has cooled and mixed.
- HVAC model issue results in mass smoke etc, to be spread ”instantly” between
nodes, with very low velocities oscillating very frequently the smoke is spread quickly
throughout the network.
The fluctuations or high frequency oscillations seen are believed to be caused by the
instantaneous mass transfer between HVAC nodes and the FDS domain. FDS version
6.5.3 added the HVAC_MASS_TRANSPORT keyword. Setting this keyword to TRUE allows
for the HVAC node model to include the transport and storage of species and energy.
The feature is still in beta testing but it could be interesting to investigate what impact this
would have on the combustion behaviour. The ability to store mass might mean that the
fluctuations could be ”dampened” as the flow would act delayed like a spring.
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure

C1_Fast.fds
The FDS input file used in case 1 to test the simple combustion model with a fast fire
growth. the geometry consits of the fire compartment connected with one other compart-
ment. The HVAC network is not fully implemented but connects the two rooms as it will
also connect the rest of the compartments in case 2 and 3.

fire' /

when the supply and exhaust fans runs with no changes in damper configuration. 10
rooms with 5 on each floor are connected with a simple network consisting of main and
branching ducts.

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
129
==PAGE:140==
C3_FC270_PL30.fds
The FDS input file used in case 3 with fan curve FC270 and vent pressure loss of PL30.
The setup has no supply fan but a dedicated smoke fan is connected using a bypass
duct. The setup is otherwise equal to case 2 but the fan curve and vent loss parameters
changed thoughout the study, resulting in 35 simulations.
&HEAD CHID = 'Case3_FC270_PL30', TITLE='Case 3 Fan curve 270 Pressure loss 30
Pa' /
---SIMULATION AND SOLVER PARAMETERS -------------------------
&TIME T_BEGIN=0, T_END=400/
&MISC TMPA=20,
P_INF=101325,
SIMULATION_MODE='VLES',
STRATIFICATION=.TRUE.,
HVAC_PRES_RELAX=1,
HVAC_LOCAL_PRESSURE=.TRUE./
&PRES CHECK_POISSON=.TRUE.,
PRESSURE_TOLERANCE=2000,
MAX_PRESSURE_ITERATIONS=20,
VELOCITY_TOLERANCE=0.01/
&RADI RADIATION=.TRUE./

Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
139
==PAGE:150==
E
Matlab script
FDS_find_LOC.m
This is the script referenced in the design fire test in case 1. It read the HRR and DEVC
csv files in a FDS working directory. It then find the stable value of the limiting oxygen
concentration and determines a point of extinction (point of oxygen depletion). The script
plots the results and prints a ramp simulating the point of extinction to the console for easy
implementation in FDS.
clc
clear all
close all
warning('off')
%% USER INPUT
% Choose the "tightness" of finding the first LOC point
% 'proc' should be between 0.05 and 0.2 (5-20%)
% this is the minimum O2 concentration found + (5-20% of the concentration)
proc = 0.10;
%% Point to a directory to scan for .csv files
path = uigetdir('Select a FDS working folder');
fileList = dir(fullfile(path,'*.csv'));
%% Look for files with the extensions _hrr.csv and _devc.csv
N = {fileList.name};
P = {fileList(1).folder};
% Assign file names to variables and print to console
X1 = ~cellfun('isempty',strfind(N,'_devc.csv'));
X2 = ~cellfun('isempty',strfind(N,'_hrr.csv'));
disp(P);
disp(['The DEVC file found is named: ',N{X1}]);
disp(['The HRR file found is named: ',N{X2}]);
disp('');
DEVCstr = fullfile(P,N{X1});
HRRstr = fullfile(P,N{X2});
% Read the data in the .csv files
[~,~,DEVCdata]=xlsread(DEVCstr{1});
[~,~,HRRdata]=xlsread(HRRstr{1});
% Seperate csv into head and data matrices
DEVChead = string(DEVCdata(1:2,:));
DEVCdata = cell2mat(DEVCdata(3:end,:));
HRRhead = string(HRRdata(1:2,:));
HRRdata = cell2mat(HRRdata(3:end,:));
% Find a column with the string 'O2'
indx = ~cellfun('isempty',strfind(DEVChead ,'O2'));
indx(1,:)=[];
%% Create a moving average of data
prompt = {'How many points would you like to average over?'};
dlgtitle = 'Moving mean';
dims = [1];
140
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
==PAGE:151==
definput = {'9'};
MM = inputdlg(prompt,dlgtitle ,dims,definput);
O2Data = movmean(DEVCdata(1:end,indx),str2num(cell2mat(MM)));
%% Find LOC and extinction time
test = min(O2Data(:))+proc*min(O2Data(:));
timeLOC = DEVCdata(find((O2Data(:)<test), 1, 'first'),1)
time = find((O2Data(:)<test), 1, 'first');
LOC = O2Data(time:end);
meanLOC = mean(LOC);
%% Plot of O2vol
figure(1)
plot(DEVCdata(:,1),O2Data(:),'r','LineWidth',1.5,'HandleVisibility','off')
hold on
plot([timeLOC ,timeLOC],[0,LOC(1)],'--k','LineWidth',1.5,'HandleVisibility','
off')
plot([0,timeLOC],[LOC(1),LOC(1)],'--k','LineWidth',1.5)
plot([0,DEVCdata(end,1)],[meanLOC ,meanLOC],'-.g','LineWidth',1.5)
hold off
grid on
box on
ax = gca;
ax.XAxisLocation = 'origin';
ax.YRuler.Exponent = 0;
ytickformat('%.2f')
pbaspect([2 1 1])
xlim([0 400])
xlabel('Time [s]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
ylabel('O2vol [mol/mol]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
legend('Extinction point',['Mean of LOC = ',num2str(meanLOC),' [mol/mol]'],'
location','northeast','FontSize', 11,'Interpreter', 'none');
%% Plot of HRR and extinction time
figure(2)
plot(HRRdata(:,1),movmean(HRRdata(:,2),str2num(cell2mat(MM))),'r','LineWidth',
1.5)
hold on
plot([timeLOC ,timeLOC],[0,max(HRRdata(:,2))],'--k','LineWidth',1.5)
hold off
grid on
box on
ax = gca;
ax.XAxisLocation = 'origin';
ax.YRuler.Exponent = 0;
ytickformat('%.0f')
pbaspect([2 1 1])
xlabel('Time [s]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
ylabel('O2vol [mol/mol]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
legend('HRR','Extinction point','location','northeast','FontSize', 11,'
Interpreter', 'none');
%% Create RAMP t-squared HRR and time vectors
tmax = 293;
seconds = [1:10:tmax];
seconds = [seconds tmax];
seconds=seconds(seconds <timeLOC);
seconds=[seconds timeLOC];
Q=0.047.*seconds.^2;
Practical issues with coupling ventilation simulations in case of fire induced pressure
141
==PAGE:152==
seconds=[seconds timeLOC+10];
Q=[Q 0];
%% Plot of RAMP
figure(3)
plot(seconds ,Q,'b','LineWidth',1.5)
grid on
box on
ax = gca;
ax.XAxisLocation = 'origin';
ax.YRuler.Exponent = 0;
ytickformat('%.0f')
pbaspect([2 1 1])
xlabel('Time [s]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
ylabel('HRR [kW]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
%% Plot of RAMP and simulated HRR
figure(4)
plot(HRRdata(:,1),movmean(HRRdata(:,2),str2num(cell2mat(MM))),'r','LineWidth',
1.5)
hold on
plot(seconds ,Q,':b','LineWidth',1.5)
plot([timeLOC ,timeLOC],[0,max(HRRdata(:,2))],'--k','LineWidth',1.5)
hold off
grid on
box on
ax = gca;
ax.XAxisLocation = 'origin';
ax.YRuler.Exponent = 0;
ytickformat('%.0f')
xlim([0 400])
pbaspect([2 1 1])
xlabel('Time [s]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
ylabel('HRR [kW]','FontSize', 12, 'Interpreter', 'none');
legend('Simulated HRR','RAMP HRR','Extinction point','location','northeast','
FontSize', 11,'Interpreter', 'none');
print(figure(1),'-depsc',sprintf('file%d.eps', 1))
print(figure(2),'-depsc',sprintf('file%d.eps', 2))
print(figure(3),'-depsc',sprintf('file%d.eps', 3))
print(figure(4),'-depsc',sprintf('file%d.eps', 4))
%% Create FDS input for the RAMP and print to console
disp('FDS RAMP INPUT:')
disp('&SURF ID=''FIRE'',RAMP_Q=''HRR_ramp'', COLOR=''ORANGE'' HRRPUA=1000,
TMP_FRONT=98.0/')
for i=1:length(Q)
disp(['&RAMP ID=''HRR_ramp'', T=',num2str(seconds(i)),', F=',num2str(Q(i)/4
000),'/'])
end
